The Americans set forth the military
evacuations meant relinquishment of
Spanish sovereignty in Cuba without
reference to the proceedings of the
commission in Paris or to the final date
set by the peace commission, and made
a second point that property described
as immovable should not be withdrawn
from the Island. In reply Sagasta says
he cannot discuss these mutters with
the Spanish commission in Havana.
They are only for the consideration of
the Paris commission. It can easily be
understood that this is a pcrlite way of
refusing to recognize the jurisdiction of
the American commission and really
places Wade, Sampson and Butler at
a distinct disadvantage. It is now for
them to formulate their policy, which,
according to all precedents, must be
answered in writing to Parrado and his
associates.
There will be more correspondence
with Madrid and further loss of time.
In the meantime this note from Madrid
gives practically to Blanco and Parrado
authority to proceed with disembarka
tion without reference to American pro
testa. Regarding the sanitary condi
tion of the city and the possibility of

sponse of the brethren.

PHILIPPINE INSURGENTS
VICTORIOUS AT ILOILO.
MANILA, Oct. 12.â Late reports from Hollo are to the effect that
the rebels have surrounded the city and are holding the Spaniards
strictly within their defenses.
The natives about Manila show no disposition to commit any
overt act against the Americans, although rumors of a Spanish cleri
cal plot have been revived.
The outbreak of smallpox is probably under control, only one new
case having been reported since Saturday.
The Scandia will be converged into a hospital ship and used to
take convalescents on short sea voyages.
port made to the department by order
of the commission sets forth the facts,
as related in a dispatch to the Herald.
The sanitation of Havana is so bad
that if given something to feed upon
the fever will probably spread with
frightful rapidity.

The grand marshal then made the

By order of the most worshipful grand

Rev. Dr. Voorsanger, orator of the
day, was then introduced and pave a
resume of the work attached to and
the history of the erection of the home.
In the course of his address he said:
All great results have been a struggle,
at most times against the consent 01 the
majority, for the world, usually indis
posed to listen to progressive ideas, will
turn its face to the suggestion that, as
time goes on, the condition of man might
improve. And our noble structure, which
we consecrate to-day amid the benisons
of 20,000 Masons, is an exception to the
rule, but in this that we may well marvel
that its erection was but the result of six
years of labor. That was owing, most
worshipful grand master, to the splendid
disposition of the craft, to the magnifi
cent ability of that noble host of women
who share their husbands' and fathers'
sentiments regarding the noble purpose
of our order. Th.it was owing to the high
motives which every Mason places before
himself and permits him to execute the
mandates of his craft not only in obedi
ence to those who command him but with
willing sacrifice, because the constitution
which he obeys teaches him naught but
good. It is a testimony to Masonic char
ity that stands here for all time. It is an
eloquent expression that the tents of the
freat fraternity which we delight to
onor are deeply rooted In this far West
ern soil, and, as it stands, it is even a
promise that, while time shall abide, Ma
sonry in California shall ever strive up
ward and forward in order that it may
testify unto the world that the highest
mission Intrusted it by the Grand Master
above is a mission of love, a mission of
peace, a mission of humanity, a mission
to extend the hand of fraternity unto all
of God's children, unto all who
confess themselves by his name, unto
all who are weary and heavy laden, unto
all who themselves are imbued with the
love of God which shines in the heart of
man.

COLONEL SMITH OVERLOOKED
MEN WHO DID THE FIGHTING
NOT ADVANCED.
Californians Gain All the Honors at
Manila, but Officers With Friends
at Washington Get the
- Benefits.
BY SOL N. SHERIDAN.
Special Correspondence of The Call.
MANILA, Sept. â The story of the
taking of Manila is a tale that is still
being told in various ways. It is being
told, for one thin- in the rewards
dealt out at Washington to the soldiers
who are supposed to be deserving of
all honor â and dealt out, so far as our
meager information goes, as rewards
are always dealt out at Washington.
Dealt out, in effect, not to the men who
are most deserving. It is a long pull
that reaches from Manila to the dome
of the Capitol. To be effective, as it
seems to have been, it must of neces
sity be strong. We have been told out
here that Anderson, Greene and Mac-
Arthur have been made major generals
for the part they took in the battle of
August 13. We do not in the least ob
ject to that. Anderson was in command
on that day, and success, however won,
goes to the commander. Also, Ander
son is a good soldier and a good fellow.
He gave the command for California to
go ahead â and California went ahead.
She always doesâ save, perhaps, when
it comes to getting the reward of merit,
the throne.
Mac Arthur is likewise a good soldier
and a good fellow, and the fight he
made on the extreme right deserved
the honor he has won.
Again, nobody will object to
Greene's advancement. Greene built
the trenches before Malate, and
the boys in the ranks will remem
ber him kindly for that. He made
the approach on the strongest front of
I the enemy's works, in the traditional
\ way, approved by engineer . officers,
! since t>;e brutal pastime of war be
came a. science. 'i he lads who tolled
at those trenches under the burning
sun or through the torrential rains of
the tropics, the brave boys who kept
long ward behind the works through
the night hours while the searchlights
from the ships swtept up and down
over them, while Spanish sharpshooters
snapped at them from clumps of bam
boo, the widows and loved ones of
those men killed in keeping the trenches
where there was need for neither the
trenches nor the keeping, the dead
asleep by the lonely Paranaque, where
no American dead had need to lie, with
the voices of the living and the voice
less eloquence of the lead, will acclaim
Major General Greene. Let us get
down, for once, to facts. There was
no more need for trenches before Ma-
late, no more need for the loss of one
American life in the operations prelim
j mary to the taking of Manila, than for
! the whole blundering series of opera
tions out here since Dewey's great vic
tory to set free a people whose sole
notion of freedom lies in license to cut
the throat of a 'captured foeman. Dewey
knew this. He knew the city was at
his mercy at any moment he wished to
take it, and he knew, also, that the
rebels, so-called, a rabble handful of
half-clad natives, held the Spaniards at
bay waiting the good pleasure of the
great Admiral. I believe, moreover,
that General Merritt knew the lives of
brave men lost before the city fell were
needlessly lost â only, Merritt and the
Admiral are too well drilled to admit
too much.
Let us return to the rewards of merit
for victory. Colonel Van Volzer of the
Eighteenth Infantry, we are informed,
has been made a brigadier general.
Colonel Van Volzer is the senior colonel
of General Greene's brigade, and was
in the trenches with his men, on the
firing line, when the assault began. He
did not stay In front, but that was not
altogether his fault. Let us go back a
little to the land operations attending
the fall of Manila. The case of General
Mac Arthur, in command of the right
wing, we will consider disposed of by
his promotion. On the left wing, Greene
in command, the First Colorado and the
Eighteenth Infantry were assigned to
the task of holding those trenches be
loved of the general. The Utah Bat
tery had its guns in place then, and
the men fought them like demons. The
First California led the reserve, with
the Nebraska Regiment and the Penn
sylvanians, who had kept the trenches
the night before, thrown back in the
order named. The Colorado Regiment
had its band out with full equipment,
with the purpose of making a theatrical
entrance into Manila. I speak of the
things I saw. When the ships ceased
firing and the order to advance was
given, the Colorado men, under Col
onel Hale, and the Eighteenth, under
Colonel Van Volzer, leaped over the
trenches and made a break across the
poddy fields and bamboo thickets for
the Malate fortress of San Antonio
Abad. This spoiled the band effect
somewhat, a rush through difficult
country not being calculated to in
spire the average army musician with
enthusiasm, but General Anderson had
ordered the Californians forward in the
meantime and Colonel Smith, whose
band had been utilized to carry mus
kets, swept down into the trenches with
his regiment, turned sharply to the
right, marched out to the beach on the
double, rounded the old iron barge
which formed the extreme right of the
American works, and carried his men
along the beach into Malate, headed
by the Colorado band, playing "Hot
Time in the Old Town To-night. 1
Colonel Hale's Colorado troops
reached Fort San Antonio Abad first
and put up the American flag in Ma
late. That, after the Californians had
brought up their band in good order,
seemed to be glory enough for Colo
rado. What had become of Colonel
Van Volzer and the Eighteenth in the
meantime, I did not have time to find
out Colonel Smith had orders to go
ahead with the First California, and
he went ahead. I went with him. The
Second Battalion of the First Califor
nia, Colonel Smith and Major Slma
leading, passed the Colorado troops at
Fort San Antonio. They fought their2
way down the Calle Real to the walls
of Manila, they saw the white flag fly
5 ing over the old town as they crossed
the Luneta, they were drawn up in
front of the northern gate of the walled
city, were fired upon and lost two men
through a stupid rebel blunder, had
passed through and left behind them
2500 Spanish soldiers in arms, before
General Greene himself caught them,
and before the Eighteenth regulars or
any other American troops were any
where in sight. The Colorado troops,
so far as anybody at the front knows,
stayed at Fort San Antonio all the bal
ance of that day. Certainly they never
got within hailing distance of the
troops actually in Manila.
General Greene himself was surprised
to find the Californians so far ahead.
"How did you get here, Colonel?"- he
asked, as he overtook the victorious
Californians before, the walled city.
"I had orders to come ahead, Gen
eral," was the answer. "I came ahead."
And Colonel Smith kept going. He
advanced along the wide drive that
sweeps around the walled city, sta
tioned his troops upon the Puente Es
pana, cutting off the business part of
the city from the citadel, and upon the
t â Â°uente Colsante, or suspension bridge,
tT:us virtually securing command of the
Pasig, and made his way thence into
the fine residence district of Mala
kanan, virtually holding Manila at his
mercy. I rode across the Puente Es
pana and down upon the Escolta when
the Californians on the bridge were in
peril, because the citizens themselves
did not then know the city had sur
rendered.
And for all this glorious achieve
ment Colonel Hale of the First
Colorado- not Colonel Smith of the
First California, mark youâ is made a
brigadier general. Colonel Hale is the
same man. it will be remembered, who
alarmed the camp on the first night of
his arrival at Camp Dewey, causing
the California boys to turn out in the
rain because he had heard the insur
gents fire a few shots at Malateâ
Colorado man not being accustomed to
shootingâ and also caused General
Greene to leave his comfortable quar
ters on shipboard and pull for the
shore. Colonel Hale said, after he had j
thought the matter over for a few days, ;
that he had been giving his men prac
tice drill. The sentry who came pant- j
ins: into the California camp to give
the alarm was about the most badly
scared man I ever saw at practice
drill. If he was acting a part, he j
missed his vocation when he became a
soldier. So did Colonel Hale, when he
sent to the California Regiment for
help because the Spaniards were "driv- j
ing in his pickets." It happened that a ;
reliable officer of my acquaintance was
at the front that night, in the Insurgent
trenchesâ for this was In the days be
fore General Greene had pushed Aguin
aldo's men aside to make room for his ;
own pet earthworksâ this officer I
told me nothing whatever had hap
pened between the Colorado outpostsâ
and those outposts were back of the
rebel trenchesâ and the place where
Aguinaldo had possibly half a hundred i
men stationed to guard his smooth
bore guns. The Spaniards were more
than usually quiet, not relishing the
notion of a fight in the rain, and the
rebels were also saving their powder.
The Colorado sentry who opened the
rehearsal, so far as anybody could see. j
had shot at his own shadow and ran \
away as fast as his legs could carry
him." It was masterly practiceâ for Col
onel Hale's men. Still, it was not kind
to keep the CalMornians in the dark j
concerning what was about to happen:
and certainly it was most unkind to
cause the boys to lose their sleep in
that way on the very first night Col< -
rado landed, and after the generous
Californians had even shared their to
bacco with thÂ«i newcomers.
Mind I have no criticism to make j
upon this kind of practice. It is mas
terly, in its way, but it is not war.
Still", if it could be carried through with
out a disturbance of the peace of one's j
neighbors in camp, I do not know of a
better way to break men in to the rude |
"alarms necessarily incident to the ex-!
istence of a condition of active hositli
ties I knew when Colonel Hale sent
over to Colonel Smith for help that
night on the plea that the Spaniard.-? .
, had driven in his pickets, there were
some persons rude enough to assert
that the Colorado man had cold feet
and was panicky, but those were sim
. ply the malicious, always to be four d
Joining in the detraction of a man
wholly and entirely great. Also, Col
onel Hale made a full and complete ex
planation of the whole episode, after
be had time to think about it, by the
practice drill story, and about head- j
' quarters they were content to let it j
go at that. Possibly it was the gener
alship shown in achieving any kind of .
acceptable explanation at that lime
which won Hale his stars, and not the
part he did not take in the real advance
on Manila.
â  Or it may be that the tardy turning :
out of the First Colorado Regiment I
on the night of the real alarm, that
awful 31st of July, when the First Cali
: fornia went down into the trenches
i through a hell of flying bullets and
bursting shells, marking its path by its
dead, to the relief of the hard pressed
Tenth Pennsylvania, won the brigade
for the Rocky Mountain colonel. It Is
: sometimes better generalship to save '
than to sacrifice men. Colonel Hale
kept his in reserve, well out of the line j
of fire that night.
Whatever it was, it was not being I
the first to enter Manila that gave
Colonel Hale his honor. History will
record that Colonel Smith of the First
California led his men, fighting every
step of the way. to the walls of the
old town. That is a fact even the "pull" ,
is not strong enough to overcome.
Whether Colonel Smith is to be a vic
tim of the ingratitude of our own be
loved republic is a matter for the fu
ture to determine. Californians, at all
events, should unite to see that justice
is done. The fact remains the fact,
and the witnesses are living. Every
man of Companies B, D, H and I of
the Second Battalion of the First Cali
fornia Regiment, General Greene him
self, the correspondent of the London
Times, Mr. F. D. Miilett, knows that
the facts of the advance upon Manila j
are as here set down. Colonel Smith
of the First California had the city
at his mercy before the formal capitu
lation, and before even General Mer
rltt's representative, Colonel Whittier,
also made brigadier, and the represen
tative of Admiral Dewey had landed
from the fleet to receive the surrender.
If there is one man in all the American
army who Reserves recogn.tion for dis
tinguished gallantry at the taking of
2Â£?nlla that .man is Colonel James F
tomith of the First California Volun
teers. â  .
SUICIDE AT CHICO.
Judge C. Or. Warren Fires a Bullet
Through His Brain.
\ CHICO, Oct. 12.â Judge c. O. Warren,
aged 59, one of Chico'a oldest citizens?,'
committed suicide in his office to-day by
c.hooting himself through the right tem
pie. The cause is not known, but close
friends say the Judge had been acting
strangely for some time past.
.Judge Warren had held the office of Dis
trict Attorney and Superintendent of
Schools of Butte County and City
Attorney of Chico, and at the
Unio of his death was Justice
of the Peace of Chico township. lie was
a member of Chi co Commandery No. 12
Knights Templar, and Chico Lodge No
68, Ancient Order of United Workmen,
and past patron of Eastern Star Lodge
of this city. He is survived by a widow.

! authority for the statement that Morris
| Park will not be cut into building lots j
I next season. It declares that the West-
I Chester Racing Association has the place
: for another year at least, and after that
I the Morrises have not decided.
Pat Nolan, once one of the most famous
j steeplechase riders in America, died last
week at Sheepshead Bay, whore he was
! engaged in the livery stable business.
j Nolan retired from the ranks of riders
J about twelve years ago.

lon Alpen's victory in the rich Stallion
stakes at Hawthorne last Saturday. He
> bet $2000 on the Fleischman colt, some of
! it for place and some for the show.

ent in Chicago.

BROWN'S SLAYER CONFESSES.
Augustine Burtenier Admits the
Killing of the Bancher.
SAN JOSE, Oct. 12.â Augustine Burten
ipr has confessed that he killed Frank
Brown. In an Interview at the County
Jail, this morning, he said:
'â¢I killed that man. He tried to catch
me."
This is the first time the man has con
sented to talk of the crime. He would
say no more and refused to make any ex
planation, dropping into the sullen, silent
mood which he continually maintains
From the evidence before the Coroner's
jury it would appear that the man was
out for a hunt when he crossed Brown's
field, at which time the killing occurred.
NO CHANCE FOR
THE PENNANT
FOR BALTIMORE
Did Not Come Up to
Expectations.
PITCHERS PROBABLY TIRED
SOMETHING MORE THAN LUCK
FOR BOSTON.
The San Franciscos Cannot Win
League Honors, but They
May Play Good
Ball.
By Joe Corbett.
The Baltimore^ did not make the cham
pionship finish which I predicted some
few days ago.
The pennant is now lost to them, yet it
cannot be said that they did not make a
remarkable run for it.
With their present pitching: staff, which
I consider the greatest in the country,
much was expected of them. That they
failed to beat Boston during the last
series, I make no doubt, was no direct
fault of theirs.
Their fight for the pennant this year in
a great many respects resembled that of
last season. Many ups and downs were
experienced at unexpected places, and
pitchers, to keep in the race, had to work
unreasonably hard, and in consequence,
I fear, were not strong when the leaders
came along.
It happened so last season, and while I
cannot say that the pitchers this year
gave out as they did last, still they were
worked hard, and very reKu.arly, which
fact in itself is sufficient cause for me to
conceive them tired and anxious for
that day to come which will bring a
much-needed rest.
The Bostons, after their superb work
this year, â¢will be looked upon as a great
team. They won so many games tho pre
vious season that peopleâ and I arrons
themâ attributed their victories to luck
and not superior playing, for the reason
tnat contest after contest was won in the
? . v V lnln&> after they appeared irre
trievably lost.
As an illustration take one of the Bal
timore s games played at Boston. For
eight innings the above mentioned collec
tion held a winning lead, and after their
| half of the ninth inning they were four
i runs ahead. The Bostons took their last
! licks, and, with tv.o men out and two
i strikes on the butter, six bases on balls
and a single followed. Enough runs were
gathered to win the game, and after it
waÃ Â°,yer "11 I could hear and say was
1 U CX a
This is but one instance of the many
games won by the Boston club last sea
son in a peculiar manner, but now that
they have again demonstrated that their
championship aspirations are genuine, It
is to be hoped that the "luck" story will
pass into oblivion.
I for one have arrived at the conclusion
and without much deliberation that the
Boston club is indeed great, and I sin
cerely trust that the Eastern supporters
will do likewise. Surely when a team
wins the national pennant twice in suc
cession there must be some other ele
ment besides luck prevailing, nnd this
element is "good baseball." True, they
have had a fair smile from the fates, but
all teams are acquainted, to no little ex
tent, with the unforeseen, and, to be
frank, what would a team do if the smil
ing fates did not occur occasionally
Without any doubt the San' Francisco
team, as it lined up on Sunday last is
very good.
Iberg, as everybody knows, is one of the
very best pitchers in the league, an i
*''i h , a R ? llly on second to strengthen the
infield I feel at intervals that they are
invlncibles.
Of course, I do not always labor under
such unique delusions, but there are mo
ments, such as the present one, in which
I permit myself to think kindly of this
famous icecream collection.
The one complaint, however, lies In the
fact that when you call the Friscos a
clever aggregation they turn right around
on their next outing and put up a foolish
exhibition. I must, however, state, de
spite the fact that I am apprehensive as
regards the next exhibition the Friscos
put up, they are at present. If appear
ances go for anything, a great team.
The team, of course, has no chance for
the pennant; but this really makes no
difference, for the "rooters" would dearly
love to see them, as their representative
club, play just a few good games before
tho season closes. That they will I am
Inclined to believe, but I will not say so,
for an old adage ha 3it that "there is
many a slip iwixt the cup and the lip."
The Friscos, as I previously mentiom-d,
play In accordance with the above say
ing, and, being cognizant of the fact, I
cannot state anything regarding them as
being positive.
If they can just cling together, now tnat
they have a presentable organization, long
enough to prove that they are real, live
players, their faults of the past will be
forgiven, and the finale, providing it be
up to expectation, applauded.
The Oaklands are making a glorious
record for themselves this season, for
sure. Not content with being among the i
lesser lights as a ball team, they sought j
to win laurels by cheating a restaurant- j
keeper In Sacramento very recently-, and j
came out of the melee with the same ,
percentage as they now hold in the league
race.
It is strange what some players will do
to exalt the game.

The Cost of Treeing Cuba.
The United States are certainly entitled to
retain possession of the Philippine Islands if
the peace commissioners so decide, for the cost
of the war runs far into the millions, and the
end is not yet. The money paid out reaches an
astonishing total. To free the stomach, liver
bowels and blood of disease, however, la not an
expensive undertaking. A few dollars invested
in Hostetter's Stomach Bittern will accomplish
the task eaeily. The poor aa well as the rich
can afford it.

VTRDEN. 111., Oct. 12.â The little town of Virden Is compara
tively quiet to-night, after a day of riot and bloodshed, resulting
from the long expected clash between the union miners and im
ported negroes. At 12:40 o'clock this afternoon a Chicago and Al
ton special train bearing 200 negroes from the south arrived at the
stockade around the Chicago- Virden Coal Company's mines and im
mediately terrific firing began. The list at 10 o'clock to-night stands
seven dead and eighteen wounded.

DECLARES MINE OWNERS
ARE GUILTY OF MURDER
Governor Tanner of Illinois Scores the
Men Who Are Importing Negroes
to Replace Strikers.
SPRINGFIELD, 111.. Oct. 12.â 1n an Interview with Governor Tan
ner this: evening regarding the Virden riot he said:
"T. C. Louck, president, and Mr. Lukens, superintendent, of the
Virden Coal Company, at 12:30 to-day, made good their threats to land
a trainload of Imported laborers from the South and attempted to put
them to work in their mines at the point of the bayonet and the
muzzle of the Winchester, such laborers being drawn largely, if not
entii the criminal '-laps, ex-convicts who learned their trade
while doing time ) n the penitentiaries of Alabama, after having been
fully advised and having full knowledge that the landing of such im
port" Tfl would precipitate a riot. I had wired them that if
they brought thes< 1 laborers they did so at their own peril,
and, under the circumstances, would be morally responsible and
criminally liable for anything that might happen. From the informa
tion I can gather at this time the very minute the train stopped in
front of the coal shaft, where the doors of the stockade were thrown
n for the imports, i laborers to enter, the firing began. The killed
and wounded are largely idle miners who were on the outside. The
ers were the hired guards who were brought along by the coal
pany. Most, if not all, of them, were non-residents of Illinois.
There Is no means of learning their names or whereabouts, for the
reason thai they d them out, knowing, perhaps, that
ihey are criminally liable for mur . r. as they had no permission from
any oflicial In limioia ......; . ./.. putizing them to act as deputy
marshals or deputy sheriffs. Instantly on learning of the trouble I
dire* te<! Adjutant General Recce to order Captain Craig of the Gales
burg Battery and one company of the Sons of Veterans Regiment,
now stationed at I'ana, to proceed at once by the quickest routes to
the scene of the trouble. General Recce accompanied Captain Craig,
and I have instructed General Recce to quell the riot and maintain
order, protect life and property, to disarm all persons bearing arms
and to not allow imported laborers to unload from any train within
the limits of the city, nor to march in a body.
"These avaricious mine owners, who have so forgotten their duty
to society as to bring about thlÃ blot upon the fair name of our State,
have gone far enough; yes, too far, as they had fair warning from
me, by wire and telephone, that importation of labor which brings to
our Slate an undesirable class of citizens had to stop. And I say
now to such and all others that this is a thing of the past; that it
. shall not be tolerated in Illinois while I am Governor. These men,
the president and officers of thlp company, who participated in this
riot n >' the bringing of imported labor, are guilty at murder,
and should, and, I believe, will be, Indicted by the Grand Jury o f
Macoupin County and tried and convicted for this heinous offense."
REPLY OF THE MINE OWNERS.
CHICAGO, Oct. 12.â President T. C. Louck of the Chicago-Vlrden
: Company, who arrived at 9:."0 o'clock to-night from the vicinity
of the trouble, made the folh wing statement to the Associated Press:
"our position has been defined right along by the press, as the
public can ascertain, and we simply desire to state that our employes
arrived ai Virden at about 12:30 to-day. We stopped the train oppo
bo that the men could go from the train Into our works,
when Immediately the mob llred from all directions, and, very natur
ally, our men defended themselves. The consequences in full we do
not know positively as yet. As to our future ac|ion, we pro-pose to
follow in the future, as we have in the past, legal procedure in the
tainlng of our legal rights, and shall take proper steps to secure
redress against all who prompted, aided, abetted or participated in
the riot of to-day, whether they are miners, miners' officials. State offi
cials or others. We shall determine before we are through whether
the government of this State can clfifs our colored population as ex
convicts, scalawags, etc., with impunity, and whether the colored citi
zens of this country can have their rights under the constitution set
HPide at the whim and pleasure of the government of Illinois. We
shall determine for ourselves and others In this State Just how far a
Governor can annul and evade the duties placed upon him by the con
stitution and statutes of this State."

To-day the Chicago and Alton lim
ited, due to pass here at 10 o'clock, shot
through en route to Chicago an hour
late, displaying flags on the rear Indi
cating that a special was following.
Immediately the word was spread and
a dense crowd of miners lined the sta
tion platform, while another collected
at the entrance of the stockade, half a
mil â  north of the station. D. B. Kiley,
a Chicago and Alton detective, stood
guard at the switch at the south end
of the station platform to see that it
was not tampered with.
At 12:40 the special train passed the
Station and signal shots were fired from
the south end of the train, announc
ing the spt-eiaJ's arrival. Immediately
shots were tired from the moving train
and outside and the battle was on. A
few moments after the train had passed
the switch where Kiley was stationed,
ami while he was talking with two citi
zens he threw up his arms and dropped
dead with a bullet through his brain.
He was the first man killed.
The train continued to the stockade.
the miners firing into it all along the
route, and the negro passengers re
turning the fire. The moment the train
reached the stockade th>- miners opened
a desperate fire with Winchesters, re
volvers and firearms <<f all descriptions.
The negroes on the train answered with
a steady fire. The miners and the train
were enveloped in a cloud of smoke
and the shooting sounded like a con
tinuous volley. Engineer Burt Tigar
THE SAN FEANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, OCTOBER 13, 1898.
received a bullet in the arm and
dropped from his seat. His fireman
seized the throttle, pulled it open with
a jerk, and the train was under sueed
carrying a load of wounded negro pas
sengers to Springfield.
How many were wounded is not
known. The train stopped at the stock
ade but two minutes. Its departure did
not cause the firing to cease. The tower
df the stockade was filled with sharp
shooters, armed with Winchesters, and
they kept up a steady fire into the
crowd of union miners. Eye-witnesses
say that miners were killed after the
train had departed. It is not known
how many men are stationed behind
the walls of the stockade, but an es
timate is placed at between twenty-five
and forty. It is claimed that six within
the stockade were wounded, but those
Inside refuse to hold any communica
tion with the outside and nothing au
thentic can be learned. Word was,
however, sent from the stockade to the
physicians In town that their services 1
werÂ» needed.
The supply and provision store of the
Chicago-Virden Coal Company is
known as the Climax Trading Com
pany, with Superintendent J. F. Eyster
in charge. At 2 o'clock, after the firing
at the stockade had subsided, an at
tack without a parallel in the history
of the trouble, was made on TCyster in
this store on Main street, one block
from the depot, which will probably
cost him his life. He was sitting in his
store when his telephone rang and he
whs instructed from the stockade to
secure physicians and hurry them to
the place. Eyster jumped into his de
livery wagon and, securing two doctors,
rfkshed them to the mines.
He returned to his store, climbed out
of his wagon and was just entering the
door, when the cry was raise,! that
Manager Fred Lukens of the miners
was with him. With a rush a throng
of infuriated miners pressed toward
the store. Eyster ran behind a coun
ter with a revolver In each hand. The
miners pressed hard after him and as
Eyster sprang upstairs he and the min
ers began shooting simultaneously. He
ran to the top of his building and
Jumped behind a chimney, while the
miners ran into the street and opened
tire on him again. Chips flew from the
brick chimney and Eyster ran from
cover across the roof of another Ptor?.,
tiring into the street below as he ran.
From there he crossed to the roof of
the Rank of Virden, where he reloaded
Ills revolvers. Blood whs flowing from
a wound in his side, but with dogged
d< t< rmination, against terrible odds, he

continued his flight. Jumping to the ]
roof of the Rae & Glsh drug store, he j
halted behind a projection from the ;
roof of the building he had Just left
and emptied the twelve chambers of'
his two revolvers.
Then, springing from the cover, Ey- j
ster dashed ahead amid a rain of bul
lets to the roof of the Steed building,
the upper story of which is known as
Miners' Hall. He either fell or Jumped
through the skylight and landed in the
arms of a crowd of miners, who seized
him and carried him downstairs to the
street. Other hands seized the almost
unconscious man and dragged him into
the middle of the street. Local police
men drove back the crowd and carried
Eyster to the city square across the
street and laid him on the grass. Oy
ster was motionless and supposedly
dead. In a few minutes he was seen
to raise his hand and wipe the blood
from his face. Two men sprang at
him, and, with the ferocity of tigers,
began jumping on his body and strik
ing him on the head with stones. With
a yell the angry crowd charged into
the square to kill Eyster. The police
charged In a body and fought their
way to the center of the mob. where
they took a stand over the prostrate,
battered, bleeding man. A carrier was
procured and Eyster was taken to the
Buckless Hotel. He had been shot
through the groin and is terribly bat
tered up about the head. The physi
cians say that he has barely a chance
for recovery.
The dead miners were removed from
the vicinity of the stockade to hotels
and livery stables, and the wounded
miners were taken on litters to the sta
tion and taken to Springfield to-night
on the 8 o'clock train.
An Associated Press reporter secured
admittance to the stockade late to
night. The list of dead and wounded
inside the stocknde follows:
DEAD.
A. W. MOROAN, Chicago.
WOUNDED.
EL Gritgesell, shot In shoulder.
O. J. Snyder, shot in face and legs.
James Sickles, Chicago, shot In leg.
Frank Wilder, Chicago, shot In arm.
Thomas Mclntee, Chicago, shot in leg.
Two doctors were at work with the
wounded and communication with them
w;is prohibited.
The stockade surrounds about twenty
acres of ground in a square and is
made of pine boards a.n inch thick, pet
edge to edge about twelve feet high. On
each of the four sides, about midway,
is a small watch tower containing a
guard armed with a Winchester.
There are three entrances, which are
closely guarded by half a dozen armed
sentries day and night. The fight to
, day occurred at the east entrance.
There are about thirty-five big. strong
men stationed Inside the stockade to
night, each keeping watch through a
loophole. The four towers have been
deserted.
Manager Lukens remained at his
desk in the office all night issuing or
ders to his men.
Manager Lukens said to-night: "The
blood of every man shed here is on the
Governor's head. He is absolutely out
side of the law and has no justification
Whatever In refusing to Bend troops.
Ills public statement that the miner
had the same right to fight for his
property, which was his labor, as the
mine owner did to protect his property,
inspired the men to the action which
they took to-day in firing upon the
train as soon as it came into our town."
To-night Ed Oreen of Mount Olive, a
union miner, died of injuries received In
to-day's shooting. This makes a total
of eight dead.
WOUNDED MEN ARE
TAKEN TO SPRINGFIELD
SPRINGFIELD, 111., Oct. 12.â The
special train on the Chicago and Alton
which brought the Alabama negroes
from Virden had eight wounded nifn,
all deputies except one, who was a col
ored miner, who were taken to the
Springfield City Hospital. Of these one
man died to-night, William W. Carroll,
a deputy sheriff. He was shot three
times, c-ne bullet passing through his
neck from the right side, another pass
ing into the temple in the right side
and the third entering the brain over
the eye, crushing the skull. Another
train, which arrived at 9 o'clock to
night, brought up six wounded men,
vyho are at St. John's Hospital. Those
at Springfield Hospital are:

will probably recover.

John M. Hunter o-f Pontiac, the presl- i

Governor Tanner to-night wired the
War Department, asking if the Fifth
Illinois Infantry could not be placed at
his disposal for use at Virden. Colonel
Culver, the commander of the Fifth,
has tendered his services and those of

MAYOR PENWELL HAS
DISMISSED THE POLICE
PANA, 111., Oct. 12.â Mayor Penwell,
who Is the son of a prominent, coal op
erator, h.is discharged the entire police
force, charging them, it is said, with
sympathy for the strikers. New men
were sworn in, but eight of the ten City
Councilmen announce that the new offi
cers will be removed at the next Coun
cil meeting.
SHOT DOWN BY MILITIA
AT THE STOCKADE
VIRDEN, 111.. Oct. 12.â A detail of
militia at 10:50 to-night killed ex-Lieu
tenant of Police Tom Preston of Chi
cago at the stockade. He was standing
outside the stockade as guard. The
militia gave the bystanding miners the
command to halt and Preston stepped
back to the gate. The militia fired and
he was shot in the stomach. He was
carried in* the offce of the stockade,
where he expired.
1 lattery D of Galesburg, m., under
Captain Craig, numbering ICO men, ar
rived here to-night from Pana.
Adjutant General J. N. Recce came
from Springfield with the militia to
night.
He said that ex-Lieutenant Preston
was not killed by the militia. He said
that when the guard at the stockade
had dodged into the entrance at the
militia order of "hands up," a revolver
shot was fired from the darkness and
Preston fell mortally wounded. Gen
eral Recce said the militia did not fire
a shot, and Preston was killed with a
revolver by some one unknown.

HOWARD GOULD
GAINS BRIDE AND
LOSES MILLIONS
Weds Miss Katherine
Ciemmons.
SACRIFICES GOLD FOR LOVE
SIMPLE CEREMONY IN A NEW
YORK HOTEL.
By the Terms of His Father's Will
the Young Man Deprives
Himself of $5,000,000
by Marrying.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Oct. 12.â Howard Gould,
by the terms of his father's will, lost
$5,000,000 to-night.
Gould and Viola Katherine Ciemmons
were married at 9 o'clock in her apart
ments at the Holland House. Rev. Dr.
Wilton Merle Smith, pastor of the
Central Presbyterian Church of West
Fifty-seventh street, performed the
ceremony.
The bridal parlors were simply but
beautifully decorated with the rarest
roses, orchids and other flowers sup
posed to exhale the sweet language of
love. The bride was exquisitely attired
in white satin, trimmed with old point
lace, the finest to be found in all Eu
rope. Otherwise there was no display.
All was simplicity and beauty. No
gems adorned the radiant woman ex
cept a small diamond pin at the throat.
She wore no flowers but a simple cor
sage bouquet. There were no brides
maids nor any one to give the bride
away.
Preparations for the wedding were
kept a profound secret. No one dreamed
that the event was to take place ex
cept the few suddenly invited guests.
They we 5 Mr. and Mrs. John Kimble,
Mr. and Mrs. G. U. Fitzpatrick, Leroy
W. Baldwin, Captain W. G. Shackford,
captain of the Yacht Niagara; W. Bo
gardus and Mr. and Mrs. Sells.
It was a notable scene, an event to
be remembered through a lifetime, be
cause it meant so much. The loss of
$5,000,000. the possibilities of litigation
and grievous heartrendlngs and cruel
family dissensions were all swallowed
up in the Joy of the occasion. Howard
d.iuld stood up like a man who was
sure he knew what he was doing, gave
his hand to his bride and the clergy
man tied the knot in the language of
the service of the Presbyterian church.
After the ceremony the newly wedded
couple sat down to a wedding supper
served with royal elegance and com
pleteness In the "gilt room." Immedi
ately after the supper Mr. Gould and
his happy bride entered their carriage
and drove to the yacht Niagara. The
yacht shot into the stream like an ar
row and headed down the coast, bound
for Washington, Old Point Comfort and
other interesting places for honey
moon and loitering.
Mr. Gould urged Miss Clemmons to
marry him more than a year ago. Im
mediately members of his family ex
pressed opposition and by innuendo and
newspaper publications Miss Clemmons
was made to feel how bitter this op
position was. In accordance with her
wishes and out of respect to her Judg
ment Mr. Gould consented to wait, but
with many protests. But at last his
importunities prevailed, the wedding
taking place to-night.
Miss Clemmons answered her perse
cutors when she began su jt against a
New York morning daily and denied
that Colonel W. F. Cody (Buffalo Bill)
had given her her dramatic education
or had advanced her a fortune. She
said he was only her dramatic agent
and as such had advanced her no funds
that had not been repaid.
Howard Gould said to-night that he
expected to return from his wedding
cruise in about three weeks. By the
middle of November he will be at work
in the swirl and roar of New York
business. Though Mr. Gould has pre
pared himself to accept the loss of %;>,
000,000, the forfeiture under the codicil
of his father's bequest, he will still have
left a snug little nest egg of $5,000,000
in his own right.

PBEPABING FOB THE FBAY.
Boss Phelan Names His Men to Di
rect the Battle for Office.
The campaign committee of Boss Phe
lan's mutual admiration society of one
hundred job-chasers met last night In
executive session. They spent three hours
In discussing plans for the campaign, but
transacted little business.
Boss Phelan experienced no email dif
ficulty in securing a campaign committee
that would "programme" on every prop
osition. Some of the job-chasers realize
thai they were used as figure heads at the
convention and in consequence they were
mad. In not a few of the districts there
were signs of open rebellion and great
tact and diplomacy had to be used in In
ducing the warriors to take off their war
paint and get back into camp. After the
pipe of peace had been smoked, the great
mogul, Phelan, through his trusted
sachem, P. H. McCarthy, announced to
the tribe that in the hands of the follow
ing braves had been intrusted the conduct
of the war for office and soft billets:
Twenty-eighth District. J. D. Maxwell;
Twenty-ninth, L. Brannan; Thirtieth, P.
J. Tomalty; Thirty-first, C. J. Gallagher
(not of the Clan Gallagher); Thirty-sec
ond J. E. Buckley (vouched for as not
being related to C. A. Buckley); Thirty
third, R. 8. Curtis; Thirty-fourth, J. H.
Hanson; Thirty-fifth, John Reedy; Thir
ty-sixth, Edward Reddy; Thirty-seventh,
W H. kead; Thirty-eighth, Charles W.
Fay (the living, moving telephone of the
boss); Thirty-ninth, J. J. Coffey; Fortieth,
6. M. Van Wyck Jr.; Forty-first, Howard
Park of Susan Forty-first, Howard
Boland (who still has hopes of some day
becoming a Supervisor); Forty-third, T.
J. Pinder (who was "killed off" by the
Examiner for Recorder); Forty-fourth,
H. L. Rea; and Forty-fifth, M. H. Her
non of San Rafael.
Boss Phelan, in order that no mistakes
be made in the campaign work, "inti
mated" that his mouthpiece, Charles W.
Fay, be elected chairman. The "inti
mation" carried, as also did the "sugges
tion" that P. I. Boland be made secretary,
whereupon Colonel "Shoot-Low" Sullivan,
who was "Invited to attend" to see that
there was no hitch in the programme like
the one. that occurred at Metropolitan
Hall, Tuesday night, applauded.
The committee then adjourned to meet
to-night, when each committeeman will
be expected to report just in what partic
ular way his district should be organized.

ENTHUSIASTIC * REPUBLICANS.
Voters in the Thirty-Ninth Assembly
District Hold a Grand Ratifi
cation Meeting.
ThÂ© Hobson Republican Club of thÂ©
Thirty-ninth Assembly District held a
grand ratification meeting Wednesday
evening at its headquarters at the cor
ner of Post and Hyde streets. Charles
A. Mau acted as chairman, James E.
Green as secretary and William Ault
mann as treasurer. There was a large
attendance, and speeches were made by
Julius Kahn, H. S. Millzner, A. J. Mor
ganstern, Frank D. Worth and many oth
ers. The following resolutions were unan
imously adopted by the club indorsing
all the Republican nominees on the State
and municipal tickets:
Whereas, The Republican party has always
consistently upheld the principles of protec
tion, freedom ami good government; and
whereas, the present administration has by its
temperate and efficient management of the war
against Spain strengthened the confluence the
people of the United States have always had in
the administration of the Government by the
Republican party; and whereas, the nomina
tions heretofore made by the Republican party
in its State, Legislative and municipal conven
tions held last month at Sacramento and in this
city have been of such a character as to pair.
the approbation and enlist the support of all
good citizens; and whereas, this club has been
organized for the purpose, among others, of
Indorsing and by every honorable means assist
ing: to elect candidates nominated by the var
ious conventions of the Republican party,
whose characters and abilities recommend them
as But table and proper persons for election,
now, therefore, be it
Resolved, That the Hobson Republican Club
does hereby indorse the platform heretofore
adopted and the candidates nominated by the
Republican conventions aforesaid, and does
pledge Itself to support the nominees of the
said conventions; and
Resolved further, That this club reiterates
its former Indorsement of the action .of this
administration in its conduct of the war with
Spain, and la in entire sympathy with the pol
icy which it has bo consistently and success
fully carried out In that behalf.
After several recitations by the mem
bers the club adjourned, to meet next
Tuesday evening, when other prominent
nominees will apear to address its meet
ing.

KAHN MAKES
ANSWER TO HIS
DETRACTORS
Rousing Rally in His
Support.
AN ENTHUSIASTIC AUDIENCE
ISSUES OF THE CAMPAIGN ARE
ABLY DISCUSSED.
Colonel T. V. Eddy, Who Has Toured
the State, Predicts a Grand
Victory for the Republi
can Ticket.
There was a grand an d enthusiastic
gathering of the friends and supporters
of Julius Kahn, the Republican candi
date for Congress In the Fourth District,
at Metropolitan Temple last night. The
utmost enthusiasm marked the proceed
ings, and the .sentiments expressed by
the speakers were greeted with cheers
that were again and a-gain renewed.
Ex-Attorney General A. L. Hart pre
sided, and after a brief and appropriate
address introduced Julius Kahn as the
next Congressman from the Fourth Dis
trict. As the candidate stepped forward
the cheers were deafening, and it was
several minutes before he was allowed to
commence his speech. He spoke of the
progress made by the country during the
McKinley administration and of the bene
fits to be derive, l by the Pacific Coast and
particularly Sun Francisco by the annex
ation of the Hawaiian Islands. He urged
that a soild Republican delegation be sent
to Congress, so that measures urgently
necessary for the progress of the State
should be Insisted upon, mentioning a ca
ble to Hawaii and the Philippines, im
provement of the great waterways
throughout the State, and locally an ap
propriation for the completion of the new
postoffice. Jn conclusion ho referred to a
personal matter as follows:
"I had hoped to conduct an entirely Im
personal campaign, but i cannot allow
the remarks made by the Governor of
this State In Woodward's Pavilion last
Friday night to go unanswered. 1 have |
lived in this community practically since
my boyhood, and during all of that time
my life has been an open book. I have
never knowingly or willfully committed
a dishonest or dishonorable act. and tne
man does not live into whose eyes 1 can
nut look with entire frankness. I have
served this city in the Legislature of tnis
State during the session of 1893, and my
fellow-citizens; Irrespective of party af
filiations, have repeatedly commended my
course in that body. The facts regarding
my employment as attorney to represent
the University of California before i^e
last Legislature are these:
â¢'ln the latter part of December, 1898.
Mr. J. B. Reins tern; one of the regents of
the university, and whose Interest in the
welfare of that institution has never been
questioned, called upon me to consult with
me regarding a number of bills affecting
the university that he desired to have
introduced In the Legislature. I freely
gave him the benefit of what little experi
ence I possessed in such matters, and
finally, about the middle of January. 1897,
he telephoned me to tome to his otllce at
once 1 went there immediately, and he then
informed me that he had called a num
ber of the regents together and they had
concluded that it would be necessary to
have an attorney upon the ground at
Sacramento during the greater Part of the
session, that they would pay $2iM> for the
work but that all expenses would have to
come out of that fee. He asked me to
accept the appointment. I declined to do
so urging the fact that I did not care to
go away from my office just at that time
or a period of two months. Ho begged
me to accept the offer, pleading that the
interests of the university demanded the
close attendance during the session of
some one familiar with legislative pro
ceedings and legislative matters. I finally
allowed myself to be persuaded to be re
tained in the case, and there was no de
ception or misunderstanding as to the
ternis of the contract. I left for Sacra
mento almost immediately and remained
there practically during the remainder of
1 '''-There "were some four or five bills or
measures, affecting the university, passed
during the session; among them was the
one which enabled Mrs. Phebe A Hearst,
whom I may call the patron saint of the
University of California, to carry out her
magnificent ideas for the construction of
entirely new buildings upon an architec
tural plan that has brought the archi
tects of the world into the competition,
and their preliminary designs were opened
at Antwerp. Belgium, only last week In
the presence of Mrs. Hearst and Mr.
Keinstein. The last bill affecting the uni
versity passed the Senate on the last day
of the Besslon and went to the Governor
for his signature on that day. Of the
J2500 about 80 per cent went for expenses.
such as printing pamphlets and circulars,
clerk hire, hire of stenographers, tele
graphing, telephoning, postage, traveling
expenses and ho on. but not one dollar of
It was used dishonestly or corruptly. If
the claim had been an unjust one, if it
1 ad been a dishonest one would men of
the standing of Judge Slack. George T.
Marye. Arthur Rodgers, Colonel C. F.
Crocker I. W. Hellman. J. West Martin,
J. B. Relnsteln. Judge Wallace. John E.
Hudd, the Governor's own brother, and
the other members of the Board of Re
rents have voted to allow the claim and
to pay It? I say again, the employment
was not of my seeking, and the fee was
not fixed by myself, and every action of
mine throughout the transaction will bear
the closest scrutiny and the most search
ing investigation. ,_ Â± ,
"No man can question the honesty of
Mr. Barry, one of my opponents. During
the same session of the Legislature a bill
was passed to pay his claim for publish
ing the constitutional amendments In his
newspaper, the Star. It was a just claim
and .vis paid, and if any friend of mine
were to try and accuse him of a wrong
ful act in taking the money that he had
honestly earned, I would not for a mo
ment hesitate In resenting tho accusa
tion." â 
In the midst of the candidate's address
the uniformed corps of the Phenix Re
publican Club marched into the hall and
lined up in front of the platform. They
called for three cheers for 'Julius Kahn,
which were heartily given. Then followed
cheers for Gaga and the Republican
ticket. They remained till the close of the
dress.
Hon. Frank McGowan was introduced
as the next speaker. He spoke eloquently
of the issues of the campaign and se
verely criticized Judge Magulre's action
in Congress in opposing the appropriation
for national defenses and the bill for the
exclusion of socialists, anarchists and
nihilists, the last named having been
classed by Magulre as the Democrats of
Russia. He spoke of Julius Kahn's do
ings while in the Legislature, and said
that his reputation was unlmpeached and
unimpeachable, and in the fullest and
grandest sense he was an honest man.
Turning his attention to James H. Barry,
he read an article from his paper which
charged President McKinley with being
in league with "Butcher" Weyler to drag
the Cubans In the dust, and made a hit
by quietly remarking, "Wouldn't he he
a daisy in Congress?" He was repeated
ly cheered for his able effort.
Colonel T. V. Eddy was the last speaker
and he roused the audience to the wild
est enthusiasm. He said he had just
come from the country districts and
brought a message from there that if the
Republicans in San Francisco did their
duty not only would Henry T. Gage be
elected Governor of the State, but the
entire ticket would be elected by a ma
jority of from 25,000 to 50,000. Judge Ma
gulre and the Examiner had departed
from the platform of the Democratic
convention and had raised a false and
fictitious issue of Collls P. Huntington
and the railroad. He defied any Demo
crat to prove that any man on thÂ© Re
publican ticket was or ever had been
under railroad influence. He closed with
a brilliant peroration depicting the suc
cess of the Republican ticket If they
would only stand shoulder to shoulder to
defeat the common enemy, for if Maguire
were elected it would drive the wheels of
time back a quarter of a century.
During the addresses some people in
the hall asked "What about Patton?" and
called forth rousing cheers. At the con
clusion of the addresses the chairman
called for three cheers for Henry T. Gage,
the next Governor, and the Republican
ticket, which were given with a will.

presidents of the meeting:

Big Republican Bally in Oakland Ar
ranged for Next Saturday.
OAKLAND, Oct. 12.â 0n Saturday night
next a big Republican rally will be held
!at the Tabernacle. The plans as already
outlined insure a great demonstration,
and with fireworks, red fire and cannon
there will be no lack of enthusiasm. W.
B. Prlngle of the County Central Commit
tee will be grand marshal of the parade.
He has issued the following orders:
All clubs participating In the Republican
procession on Saturday evening are requested
to pay strict attention to the following- in-
Btrucions: _
FORMATION OF PARADE.
Alliance Club will assemble at headquarters
and be ready by 7 o'clock sharp to march. The
Fifth Regiment band will Join the club. From
Twelfth and Webster streets the organization
will march-a long Twelfth street to Broadway;
north on Broadway to Fourteenth; west along
- Fourteenth to Washington street, where it will
! halt.
Young Men's Republican League will assem
ble at headquarters and be ready to fall in at
7 o'clock sharp, forming In single file, on west
. side of Washington street, facing east.
Pondlnky Club will form at 7 o'clock sharp.
In single file, on east Bide of Washington
street, facing west.
Other clubs will form In fours promptly at
1 o'clock on the north aide of Fourteenth street,
facing east.
ORDER OF PARADE.
Grand marshal and aids; mounted police:
I Fifth Regiment band; Republican Alliance
i Club; carriages containing speaker of the even
ing and reception committee; Pondinky Club;
other Republican organizations.
LINE OF MARCH.
On forming the clubs will march south on
Washington to Ninth- street; thence east along
Nir.th street to Broadway; thence south along
Broadway to Eighth street, where a halt will
i be made.

At 621 Bryant street iast evening the
Democrats of the Thirty-socond Assem
bly District met to hear speeches by lo
cal candidates and James H. R-irry, the
Democratic nominee for Congrvss from
the Fourth District. Herman Goodstadt
made a strong speech in favor of his
friend, Mr. Barry, after which that gen
tleman took the floor. He paid brief but
kindly respects to his Republican oppo
nent, Julius Kahn.
Democrats Organize.
The Democrats of the Forty-secamd As
sembly District organized last evening
at 310 CFerrell street. Before organiz
ing James H. Barry addressed those
present. He denounced Joseph P. Kelly,
the Independent Democratic nominee, as
a corrupt tool of the railroad.

DEATH BLOW
TO THE BOSS
Republicans to End
Phelanism.
SERIES OF DEMONSTRATIONS
MASS MEETINGS TO BE HELD IN
EACH ASSEMBLY DISTRICT.
The Revolt of Democrats Against the
Mayor Spreading Daily and
Will Engulf the Entire
Ticket.
The. Republican County Committee Is
preparing to strew more thorns In the
path of Boss Phelan and his subordinates,
and bt :Y>re November 8 the man who
wears the Democratic party under his
Bilk tile will find it would be well to follow
the example set by some of his predeces
sorsâ Buckley, for instance â and not at
tempt to be a buss and seek office at the
Bame time.
The Republican committee met yester
day afternoon and resolved to take every
advantage of the revulsion of feeling that
has set in against the Mayor. From now
on the campaign will be waged with in
creased vigor, and among the plans in
view is one that includes the holding of
eighteen big demonstrations, one Ju each
Assembly district in the cty, at which all
the municipal candidates will appear and
thus become acquainted with all the vot
ers in the city. The dates for these meet-
Ings have not been decided upon yet, but
It is the expectation to begin them next
Monday night, which would give just
eighteen days before election, not includ
ing Sundays.
These mass meetings are to be held un
der the auspices of the county committee
and will be entirely independent of any
meetings that may be arranged by the
State committee, although a conference
between the two bodies will be held and
an arrangement perfected whereby there
will be no eoniiiet in dates. Of course,
if Mr. Gage or any others of the State
nominees are in town and wish to speak
at the district meetings; the county com
mittee will cordially welcome them.
These meetings will also be independent
of the club meetings that are now being
held and will continue all trie close of the
campaign, but the clubs in each district
are expected to attend their district meet
ing in a body.
Another event will be a grand ratifica
tion meeting under the auspices of all the
clubs in town at some date in the near
future to be decided upon by the county
committee. This meeting will be held in
some central locality, possibly the Me
chanics' Pavilion, and promises to be the
largest gathering of the campaign.
The members of the county committee
aro highly elated over the present out
look ami declare that the Republican mu
nicipal ticket will sweep the city, with
possibly one or two minor exceptions.
They say the revolt of the people against
Phelanism has grown to such proportions
and the sentiment against the boss is so
bitter that he will drag the entire Demo
cratic ticket down to defeat with him.
Certain it Is that every candidate on the
Republican ticket is receiving pledges of
Democratic support in large blocks, every
day.

One Independent Has
Made Trouble.
SAWYER A TRANSGRESSOR
JOHNNY TEAGUE SAYS KIND
WORDS OF METCALF.
An Incident at Lorin That Is Evi
dence of a Need for Much
Shrewd Diplo
macy.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
i Broadway, Oct. 12.
Tho platform of the independents has
proved to be not sufficiently expansive and
clastic. It is a pretty broad platform,
but modern political history has not yet
recorded a buc :essful attempt on the part
of any man to build a platform that can
support Populists of two kinds, Demo
crats of two ways of thinking, Republi
cans of divided opinions and Prohibition
ists. Tills is what was attempted by the
im committee of the Independents,
ami it has partly failed.
The meeting at Lorin last night, at
which the independents made speeches,
was a success from some points of view,
and drew out a good-sized crowd, but
there was a little friction of a nature that,
unless great diplomacy be practiced in
the next three weeks, will seriously hurt
the chances of the independents.
K. W. Sawyer, who has been nominated
by the fusionlsts and indorsed by the in
dependents, and who first came into pub
lic notice as a Prohibition candidate, made
a speech in which he transgressed the
unwritten law of the Independents. It
w&s mutually agreed in the party that it
would support the entire biate Republican
ticket and that it only jumped the traces
in local Republican nominations because
certain bosses had done business in Han
Leandro. Much surprise wn-H, therefore,
shown when Mr. Sawyer undertook to
criticize and berate Victor H. Metcalf,
one of the leading attorneys of the Cali
fornia bar and Republican nominee f<>r
ress from this district. Mr. Sawyer
did not get very fur before be was re
minded that he was transgressing the
rules of the party whose Indorsement he
secured. Then up jumped Johnny Teague.
Teague has been a stalwart Republican
for thirty years, has held office and al
ways ran ahead of his ticket. Mr. Teague
called Mr. Sawyer to order very promptly.
â¢1 have known Mr. Metcalf for twenty
years " said 'league, '"and have always
found him to be straight goods. II- never
has wavered in his allegiance to Repub
licanism, and is a loyal supporter of
everything that McKinley stands for.
When we started this campaign it was
agreed that candidates should confine
themselves to matters r.f public Import
and should not say anything against their
competitors. We presume to be more
honest In politics than the men whom we
inxlous to defeat, and the public is
the best judge of their respective merits.
Mr TeagSe's Ben timents were very
clearly those Âf the audience, and it Is
laid that Mr Sawyer will not be again
fnvlted I to speak from the Independent
P LasÂ°t r niWht the Republican county Candi
das, with the Young Men's Republican
League's musical attachment stormed
the quiet town of Sunol and held a warm
meeting. To-night they will descend on
Niles, and preparations have been made
for a great tlnv-. _
Politics in Oakland.
OAKLAND. Oct. 12.-The Socialist La
bor party held a meeting this evening
at the Tabernacle. Job Harriman, the
candidate for Governor, delivered the
chief address and used stereopticon views
to illustrate his ideas. :
The Republicans held a rally to-night at
Hansen's Hall in West Oakland, and sev
eral of the county candidates spoke
The Third Ward Republican Club held a
largely attended meeting last night, and
speeches were delivered by Hon. F. W.
Leavitt, A. A. McKeen. William Hen
nessey. Maurice Flynn and others Joseph
Silva has been elected secretary in place
of D. J. Mullins.
A regular meeting of the Army and
Navy Republican League will be held at
their headquarters at Becker's Hall next
Friday evening.
Continental League Meeting.
A meeting of the Continental League was
held last night at Saratoga Hall on Geary
street. A large representation of its mem
bers were present and the evening was
devoted to the discussion of Republican
politics. Among the prominent speakers
were Oscar Lewis and H. C. Firebaugh.
The league will hold a big mass meeting
in its hall Hume night next week. A
â¢ committee consisting of Amos Currier,
! Frank B Worth and William McKensey
was appointed to manage the affair and
make it a success.
A Thomas F. Graham Club.
A Thomas F. Graham club was organ
ized at 529 Filbert street Tuesday even
ing. The following named officers were
elected: Charles Sommerlad, president;
William J. Splnetti. vice-president: George
A. Bardet, secretary: John Derm. sergeant
at arms. The roll was signed by 286 vot
ers.
THE SXN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, OCTOBER 13, 1898.
PILLAGERS READY
TO MAKE PEACE
Powwow Held at the
Hostile Camp.
BRAVES IN HIGH FEATHER;
SAY THEY WERE HAVING FUN
WITH THE WHITES.
Assert That Not One of Their Num
ber Was Killed or Wounded
in ' the Recent
Fighting.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
ST. PAUL. Oct. 12.â A Walker (Minn.)
special to the Dispatch says: The Bear
Islanders, otherwise the Pillager In
dians, will surrender and war has been
averted. It only remains for the terms
to be arranged before the hostlles will j
come into the agency. j
Father Aloysius Homanutz, Gus H.
Beaulieu and Chief Gay-Gwa-Che-
Way-Blmung, the peace commissioners, j
who left on the Flora last evening for
the hostile camp, got back at 4 o'clock |
this morning. They reached the camp j
at Black Duck Point at the mouth of |
the Boy River, at 9 o'clock last night, j
Indians on watch responded to signals j
and the three commissioners went !
ashore in a skiff, carrying flour, bacon, |
sugar, rice, tobacco and canned goods. i
They met a large number of the Indians
back in the woods and were given a i
cordial greeting. Hands were shaken j
all around and the battle of a week ago
was talked over. The Indians alluded
to it as a "little fun." They asserted !
that none of their number were killed
or wounded.
Bug-Ah-Mah-Ge-Shig was not at the
conference, but his greetings were
shouted across the little bay between
him and the commissioners. He waited
all day for the emissaries to appear and
left for his house across the bay soon
after dark. When the boat got in sight
the Indians sent up skyrockets. After
landing a big fire was built and a con
ference held for three hours.
The Indians were familiar with every
movement of the troops. They asked
why newspaper men had not come out
and said they had expected them. When
it was explained that it was thought
advisable for only a few people to go
out the hostlles laughed at the fears
of the people and invited the corre
spondents to go out to-day. A report in
writing, to be sent to Indian Commis
sioner Jones, was received at a late
hour to-day. The emissaries refused to
make public their report until it had
been submitted to Commissioner Jones,
further than to say that the Indians
will come to the agency when a repiv
Is sent to the statements made by them
through the commissioners.
Mr. Beaulieu says that the council
last night lasted three hours. Chief
Gu-Ne-Won-Aush was very bitter in
denunciation of the manner In which
the Chippewas of Minnesota and Da
kota were being robbed by the lumber
men. The principal cause of the diffi
culty, he said, was not whisky nor the
arrests, but the manner in which the
Indian pine lands are being: stolen.
They had vainly appealed to Washing
ton for several jtars and had finally
taken matt* is In their own hands.
Indian Commissioner Jones left to
day to attend a conference with the
Pillagers. He was accompanied by the
members of the peace party which re
turned this morning with an offer from
the Indians to lay down their arms, but
asking a hearing of their grievances.
Commissioner Jones returned to the
agency this evening, having arranged
for a council at the agency to-morrow,
to which the Indians wll send a delega
tion. The Commissioner said:
"I think this matter can be settled
In a short time."
AGNEWS ATTENDANTS
INJ FIST FIGHT
SAN JOSE, Oct. 12.â The attendants at
Agnews Asylum are having more trouble
among themselves. On Friday N. E.
Biggy and Paul Pickertag engaged in a
fist fight, and both have been suspended
until an investigation can be held. The
matter was reported at to-day's meeting
of the Board of Managers. Pickering
was the only combatant present and af
ter he told his story to the managers the
case went over without a date being set
for the hearing.
There are several factions among the
attendants and there has be-on more or
less trouble evor since the big- whitewash
ing at the institution several months ajco
This culminated in the fight on Friday.
Pickering had occasion to telephone to
Biggy in one of the wards that day, and
Hlggy could not be found. Pickering re
ported this to Dr. Sponogle.
Later in tho day liiggy and Pickering
met. The former accused Pickering of
having run to Sponogle with tales. Pick
ering called Uiggy a liar, whereupon
Biggy hit him on the eye. The men
mixed things and before they could be
separated liiggy had given Pickering a
good thrashing. Dr. Sponogle then sus
pended both men until the meeting of the
board.
Yesterday Biggy and Pickering met in
a Second street saloon in this city and
reni wed their quarrel. Bystanders inter
fered and prevented a personal encoun
ter. Pickering then went before Justice
Klrkpatrick and had Biggy arrested for
battery. He was released on bail.
Uiggy Is a brother of Registrar Biggy
of San Francisco and was placed in the
asylum by Gould. Pickering is a rela
tive of Trustee Curnow. Both men will
probably be dismissed.
SAILED UNDER
SEALED ORDERS
Speculation as to the Objective
Point of the Battleships
lowa and Oregon.
NEW YORK, Oct. 12.â The bat
tleships Oregon and lowa sailed
to-day with sealed orders from
Washington. There has been
considerable speculation as to
the destination of the battle
ships. It was first stated that
they would proceed direct to Ma
nila, but subsequent reports
threw some doubt upon this, and
Honolulu may be their objective
point, though from there they
may proceed to Manila to
strengthen Admiral Dewey's po
sition.
The United States colliers
Scandia and Celtic followed
the battleships out to sea.

MRS, ALLBRECHT
NUMBER THREE
Architect Claimed by
Another Woman.
WEDDED A SAN DIEGO MISS
SHE IDENTIFIES A LIKENESS
IN THE CALL.
Declares That She Was Deserted by
the Man Now Awaiting Trial
in banta Rosa for
Bigamy.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA ROSA, Oct. 12.â Another sen
sation was developed here to-day in the
case of Charles Allbrecht, the San
Francisco architect, at present occupy
ing a cell in the County Jail and the ,
attention of the courts. A few weeks
ago Allbrecht was arrested on a charge
of having obtained goods by f-alse pre
tense, he having issued a fraudulent
check upon the First National Bank of
San Francisco to Brooks & Loomls, a
dry goods firm here, in payment of his
bride's trousseau.
The story of his arrest and the de
scription of the man published in the
San Francisco papers attracted the at
tention of a young lady he had married
about a year before in Oakland and as
a result papers which had been lying
in the desk of the Deputy Sheriff there
for several months because of inability
to locate Allbrecht were sent to Santa
Rosa and served at once. Allbrecht
was next charged with bigamy, after
which wife No. 2 began a suit for di
vorce. A few days afterward a charge
of perjury was lodged against him and
the officers began to think things would
quiet down in his line for a time at
least.
To-day's mail brought information
which shows that still other preempts
are to be heard from. In much the
same manner in which the wife known
till to-day as No. 1 of Oakland learned
of her truant husband's whereabouts
a previous wife living in -San Diego has
located him, and from her letter re
ceived by Deputy District Attorney
I>eppo to-day facts are gleaned which
show that thp ambitious architect is at
present the husband of at least three
women.
The letter states that five years ago
the writer, then known as Anna Chris
tianson, was married to Allbrecht in
Pan Diego and as yet has never been
divorced. The description tallies ex
actly with that of the man in jail here,
and the writer states that she recog
nizes the picture published in The Call.
She says there is no doubt as to the
man's identity.

â¢ "We have failed in securing freight i

FUNDS FOR THE
BOYS OF THE
GALLANT FIRST
State Red Cross So
ciety Cables $1700.
THE HOSPITAL WAS DEPLETED
MORE MONEY WILL BE SENT
WHEN IT IS NEEDED.
j
The Soldiers Find It Much Harder
to Withstand Manila's Climate
Than the Spanish
Bullets.
Medicines, dainties for convalescent sol
diers and a whole lot of now hospital
equipment are needed by those heroes of
the conquest of Manila, the boys of the
First California. They have found it a
harder thing to stand up smilingly
against ravages of the inhospitable Phil-
Itppine climate than against the fierce
fusillades of Spanish bullets, and as a
consequence the regiment hospital and
pharmacy are depleted *J! the things most
net-ded.
On Monday a cablegram arrived at the
headquarters of the State Red Cross So
ciety from (>. H. J. Sc-hlott, the society"?
! principal ag.^nt in Manila, setting forth
the needs of the regiment and asking for
funds with which to provide them.
The response of the society was sub
stantial and prompt. It was nothing
more nor less than a cable order for
JlOOo, and Instructions to Mr. Schlott to
draw upon the London and Shanghai
Bank, at Shanghai, for 1700 more which
! is on deposit there.
This amount will be more than enough
. to provide the. boys of the gallant First
i with the things they need, and, as Mrs.
John B. Harrington said last night, "Just
< as soon as those boys want more they can
; have it if they will only let us know."
The funds sent Mr. Schlott will be
i used in the big Red Cross Hospital that
' has been established near the camp of the
First. The needs of the regimental hos
i pital will probably be taken fare of by
( the local society, if the Government does
S not in the meantime provide for its own.
GOING TO MANILA.
Major-General Merriam Formally
Assigns the Troops for the
Senator.
The formal order for the troops to em
bark upon the Senator was issued from
Department Headquarters last night. As
foretold in The Call they are the Third
Battalion of the Twenty-third Infantry,
the recruits of the Second Oregon Regi
ment, one hundred mâ¢ and two officers
of Battery D, California Heavy Artillery;
Second Lieutenant W. C. Cannon of the
Volunteer Signal Corps, Assistant Sur
geons Ira B. Ladd, C. J. Bartlett and
Bruce Foulkes, and seven men of the
hospital corps, making in all 771 men and
officers that will sail on the transport.
The guard for the Senator was detailed
from the Twenty-third Infantry, and
went aboard the vessel late yesterday aft
ernoon.
The Seventh California Regiment will
march from the Presidio at 8 o'clock this
morning. The departing soldiers will be
accorded every honor by their comrades
who remain behind. Yesterday General
Miller Issued an order for the i irst Brig
ade to form on Lombard avenue, and Col
onel Funston a similar one to the Second
Brigade. The turning out of the two brig
adea is a token of respect to Colonel
Berry and his command. General Miller
also Issued yesterday a farewell address
to the regiment. In which he expressed
his high admiration of the faithful man
ner in which the regiment had performed
the arduous duties of camp life. He also
spoke of the friendly feeling that had al
ways existed between himself and tne of
ficers of the regiment. He assured the
regiment that he entertained a high re
gard lor it for Its general efficiency, and
for /the patriotic spirit that prompted it
to Answer its country's call, and that he
would always have only the most pleas
ant memories of the Seventh California.
Private "Tony" Kiesen, Battery I, Third
Artillery who died at the post hospital
on Tuesday night, was buried yesterday.
His death was due to typhoid fever.
Lieutenant Colonel Wagner, recently
promoted to the Fourth Cavalry, has had
his sick leave extended for twenty days
and will not report until the 25th of this
month. Major Morton, also recently pro
moted to the Fourth, is expected to re
port dally. ______________
BISHOPS DECIDE TO
MEET IN THIS CITY
So the Episcopal Convention of 1901
Will Come to the Golden
West.
â WASHINGTON. Oct. 12.â The House of
Bishops of the Episcopal church to-day
voted to hold the convention of 1901 at
San Francisco.
The deputies to-day, after a long dis
cussion, adopted the following article:
"Dioceses and missionary districts may
be united into provinces in such manner,
under such conditions and with such
powers as shall be provided by canon of
the general convention; provided, how
ever, that no diocese shall be Included
In the provinces without Its consent."
Article S of the proposed new constitu
tion relating to the qualifications 6t
priests and deacons necesary to examin
ation and consecration was then taken
up. An amendment proposed by Rev.
Dr Easter of Los Angeles was adopted,
providing that in the declarations of be
lief required of bishops, priests and dea
cons, the word "doctrines," shall be in
the singular form as representing
the fundamental principle of belief
of the church, and issued to the plural
form, which sets forth its teachings.
A message was received from the House
of Bishops announcing the selection of
San Francisco as the place for noldlng
the council of 1901.
The Bishops spent the entire afternoon
in consideration of the canon relating to
the re-marriage of divorced persons, but
without finishing It adjourned until to
morrow.
Incidental to the convention was a meet-
Ing to-day of the American Church Build
ing Fund Commission. The annual report
showed that Its fund amounts to $343,890,
an Increase during the last three years of
J68.000.
A motion that the scope of the fund be
enlarged so as to loan money to erect rec
tories and school houses was referred to
the board of missions. The officers elect
ed include the following: President,
Right Rev. John Williams; vice presidents,
Right Reverends Henry B. Whlpple, "Wil
liam C. Doane. George De N. Gillesple,
Thomas A. Starkey, Cortlandt White
head and Bishops Johnson of Los Ange
les Nichols of California and Talbot of
Central Pennsylvania: secretary general,
James G. Wilson; corresponding secre
tary, Rev. J. Newton Perkins; treasurer,
George C. Thomas of Philadelphia; and
assistant treasurer, E. "W. Roberts of
New York; also a board of seventeen trus
tees.
