ORPHANS ARE ENRICHED.
Solano Court Has Decided That Good
Templars' Home Is Entitled to
a Fortune.
A decision was rendered In the Superior
Court of Solano County on Tuesday
which sustains the provisions of the will
made by the late E. A. T'pham and de
chires that the Good Templars' Home at
Vallejo is entitled to claim any property
bequeathed to it by the testator.
In September. 189T, Upham died and dis
posed of an estate of $200,000 by willing
one-third of it to the Good Templars'
Home and the remainder to his nieces
and nephews, children, of J. M. and L. M.
I'pham. Two brothers of the deceased
soon afterward brought suit to recover
the entire estate on the ground that the
provisions of the will wore illegal. In
denying their claim Judge Buckles said:
"After a careful reading of the will,
and applying the law as I understand it
to be in this State, there seems to me to
be no legal objection to the bequests and
devices made by the testator. Neither the
device to the children of .1. M. or Li. M.
Upham nor to the Orphans' Home is in
violation of any law in this State or
contrary to any decision in the Supreme
Court. The manifest intention of the tes
tator is clearly seen, and is capable of be
ing fully executed by the executors.
Therefore, and as the applicants are not
devisees and the whole estate being dis
posed of by will, the applicants are not
entitled to a partial distribution."
Judge J. M. Walley and R. Thompson
conducted the case for the Orphans'
Home.
MATRIMONIAL FRAUDS.
Isaac W. Bain and Mrs. Grace Allison
Held for Trial for Defrauding
Bomantic Grangers.
The preliminary examination of Isaac
W. Bain and Mrs. Grace Allison on a Fed
eral Indictment, charging them with run
ning a bunko matrimonial bureau, was
begun yesterday before United States
Commissioner Heacock. J. L. Duprey of
"Winters, Yolo County, took the stand and
told how, yearning for a wife to console
him with lier love and a little money, ho
had answered the gold-brick advertise
ment of the Bain Bureau and entered into
correspondence with Mrs. Allison, who
was represented to be a widow with $35,
000 in cash, just pining for a nice old
rancher to spend it for her. He made her
â i present of a $500 piano and valuable jew
elry and found that the lady did not love
and that he had gone up against a braeÂ©
him and that he had gone up against a
brace game.
William E. Looney. an ex-schoolmaster
from Kansas by way of Skaguay. narrat
ed how he had paid Bain $5 to be intro
duced to the widow and how. after two
unsatisfactory interviews, he had ceased
to woo her. He wanted consolation and
money too.
Rancher Duprey laughed heartily at the
testimony of Schoolmaster Looney. and
the latter was equally amused listening
to the rancher's story.
Commissioner Heacock held the defend
ants to answer with bail at 51000 each.
They at once furnished cash bail In the
sum of $2000 and were released.
MUST REFUND THE MONEY.
Judge de Haven Decides That Wil
liam J. Bryan Must Refund the
Money Stolen by His
Subordinate.
The United States Court of Appeals yes
terday affirmed the judgment of the Cir
cuit Court in the case of the United States
against William J. Bryan, as principal,
and Jesse D. Carr, W. F. Herrin and oth
ers, as executors of the will of W. W.
Stow deceased, to recover $93ii9 and inter
est from April 30. 1592.
The money was embezzled by Clerk
Kennedy of the Money Order Department
in the S~?.n Francisco postoffice during tho
administration of William J. Bryan as
Postmaster. Kennedy was convicted and
spent a termÂ»in the State Prison for thnt
offense. Bryan refused to make good the
amount stolen by the clerk on the ground
that Kennedy was not an appointee of his
and that he was under the civil service
system. The Circuit Court held that
Bryan was responsible for the acts of his
official subordinates, whether appointed
by him or serving under the civil service
system.
The decision of the Court of Appeals
rendered yesterday by Judge de Haven
was that the Postmaster was responsible
for the money of the office and that it was
his duty to exercise official supervision
over the clerk to ensure that all the
money was accounted for.

A Chinaman's Suit.
Yee Man, a native of the Flowery King
dom, has sued Margaret J. Morffew, and
all people holding an interest in a lot
and building at the southwest corner of
Washington street and Waverly place, to
recover $5000 damages for personal in
juries. Yee alleges that on June 13 last,
while he was passing the building in
question a large awning fell and severely
crushed him.

collateral Inheritance tax to be paid by

the estate, into the treasury. The court
finds that each must pay $20,729 56.

FOR COUNTY AUDITOR.
rOR the responsible office of County Auditor
the Republican party presents as its candidate
a thoroughly representative business man â Mr.
Asa R. Welis. This is one of the strongest and best
nominations of the year, and it cannot fail to have
the commendation and support of all citizens who
desire to provide for our county affairs an adminis
tration of sterling integrity and high efficiency.
Mr. Wells has long been identified with the busi
ness interests of San Francisco. He is one of the
leaders in our industrial and commercial activities,
and has for years taken a prominent part in the ad
vancement of the community. He has not, however,
confined his thoughts or his energies to business life.
He is one of those broad-minded, public-spirited men
who take a keen interest in the politics of the city,
State and nation, ar.d who work as energetically for
the public good as for the upbuilding of their private
fortunes. He has been an honored and sagacious ad
viser in Republican councils, has been zealous in sup
porting its principles and its candidates in every cam
paign, and. while meriting the suffrages of his fellow
citizens generally by reason of his fitness for the
office to which he ha* been nominated, has a particu
larly strong claim upon all loyal Republicans.
The importance to the county of a m2n of stanch
integrity and sound business judgment in the office of
Auditor is recognized by all who have any knowledge
of the duties of the office. Mr. Wells, by his natural
aptitude for matters of finance and the supervision of
accounts, and by the experience of his business train-
Ing, is peculiarly well fitted to render the highest ser
vice to the community in that position.
It may be accounted a good fortune that the county
has the opportunity of obtaining such a man for the
office. Hi? nomination is in accord with the general
desire to have business men of standing and repute
take charge of the affairs of the municipality, and
he ought, therefore, to receive the votes of all who
wish to bring about in San Francisco honest poli
tics, good government and municipal reform ;n; n the,
direction of honesty, economy and efficiency.
General Merritt will be pained to know that the
Spanish do not like him.
APHORISTIC MAGUIRE.
THE fusion papers in the country have broken
out with a smallpox of '"Aphorisms by Ma
guire." These aphorisms relate to equal rights,
special privileges, etc.. and are mainly extracted from
I etches in favor of land confiscation.
His diatribes against land-owners are*usual!y in
troduced by references to the rights oJ "God's chil
dren" and denunciations of monopoly. The fusion
papers print these texts or introductions, but care
rom exposing anything that follows in
the sennoi ; would find Maguire denouncing
their country subscribers, the rural land-owners, as
"cunning robbers, who have despoiled the weak and
undesigning." as "sordid extortionists" who make
"God's children pay tribute to them for the privilege
of inhabiting God's earth," and as wrongdoers
whose "investment in land must be destroyed." who
may "complain that to destroy it is a hardship, and
so it will be. but it .vill be just. "
The Placer Herald in its last issue quotes this
aphorism: "Give to all citizens equal rights in all
that constitutes the country, and disaffection, the germ
of dissolution, can never live within our countn'.
You cannot conceive of a time when, under such a
recognition of equal rights, sedition could be fo
mented or organized. â Hon. James G. Maguire.
speech on 'Free Homesteads,' House of Representa
tives, March 10, 1898."
If the Herald will print all of that speech on "Free
Homesteads" it will find it to be a declaration of war
against land-owners, whose lands, it declares, must be
taken from them to be given as free homesteads to
"God"? children.' In that speech Maguire said:
"Equity does not permit property in land; for
if one portion of the earth's surface may become
fully the possession of an individual and may be
held by him for his sole use and benefit, as a
thing to which he has an exclusive right, then all
portions of the earth's surface may be so held and
our planet may thus lapse into private hands.
Whether it may be altogether expedient to admit
claims of a csrtain standing is not to the point.
We have nothing to do with considerations of
conventional privilege or legislative convenience.
We have simply to inquire what is the verdict of
pure equity in this matter. And this verdict en
joins a protest against every existing pretension
to individual ownership of the soil and dictates
the assertion that the right of mankind to the
earth at large is still valid, all deeds, customs and
laws notwithstanding. Not only have present land
titles an indefensible origin, but it is impossible
to discover any mode by which land can become
private property. The world is God's bequest to
mankind. Ail men are joint heirs to it. You are
among the number, and because you have taken
up residence upon a certain part of it, subdued,
cultivated and beautified that partâ improved i%
as you say â you are net therefore warranted in
appropriating it as your piivate property. At
least, if you do so, you may at any moment be
justly expelled by the lawful owner, society."
There is tl* preached Ml lire in his
eh on "Free Homesteads." House of Representa
rch io. 1898." from which the Placer Her
rades ts text We invite the Herald
to print it all. and see how the land-owners who have
: beautified the Gateway from
Rocklin to Cisco like it. They will all declare that
the Government sold the land to them or their
grar.: that they ] . : their money for it and it
is theirs. In the same speech Maguire answers them.
He says: "How can this be done since we have sold
the land? Why. we sold it subject to the unlimited
right of taxation, reserved by the Federal and State
Gove- Exercise that right by concentrating
â¢ xes on lar.d values, and the only incentive men
>.aye to own land will be instantly taken away."
So the land-owr.ers of Rocklin, Loomis, Penryn,
Newcastle. Auburn. Appiegate and Coifs x. and the
ers of lowa Hill, Forest Hill and Dutch Flat, are
to be robbed of their holdings "instantly" by the
tax.
Will r e Herald print the speech and see how they
feel about this recipe for curing "disaffection"?
Chief Justice Marshall said, truly, that "the power
to tax is the power to destroy." It has been so used
-ants in all ages. Revolutions have been caused
by destructive exercise of the taxing power. Ma
guire simply proposes to use the taxing power to
destroy private ownership of land, to destroy in
srart'.y every dollar invested in land.
The Herald has taken up this issue by printing the
text, the aphorism, of the speech. It cannot decently
avoid printing the speech.
f?N EXAMPLE FOR FITZGERALD.
1 1 X PRESS companies in this country are not su
preme. This view may appear to President
- â * Valentine of the Wells-Fargo to be ultra
radical, but the law sustains it.
A test case was recently brought in the Circuit
Court of Michigan, and the Attorney General of that
commonwealth did not consider it beneath his dig
nity to appear for the people. He not only made an
argument, but his views wen sustained, and the man
damus prayed for was ordered to issue. The effect
of this was to make the American Express Company
receive and forward packages and affix to the receipts
a revenue stamp paid for by itself.
Out here the Welis-Fargo Company has made con
tentions similar to those of the American. It has
forced the customer to pay for the stamp, notwith
standing the specific provision of the statute to the
contrary. In most instances the imposition has been
submitted to. because the patron had no time to
bring the matter to an issue. In the solitary excep
tion the company was beaten, and appealed the case,
for delay to it is almost as good as actual triumph.
Attorney General Fitzgerald should imitate the
excellent example set by the Michigan official.
There can be no doubt as to the result. The Wefls-
Fargo people not only would be obliged to accept
packages and pay for the necessary stamps, but as
a common carrier, subject to wholesome regulation,
woftld not dare to make an extra charge for the ser
vice. The common carrier has a distinct place in
law. The conditions which obtain between private
parties to a contract are absent when a common car
rier is concerned.
Mr. Fitzgerald should at once proceed against the
Wells-Fargo Express Company. The law as well as
justice would be on his side, and he would have the
backing of a clearly defined and unassailable pre
cedent.

clines to talk. For once since coming to this coast
Blame seems to be displaying a streak of sense.
At least one army doctor has felt the force of inves
tigation and been bounced from the service. His
name is Tabor, and his temper is bad.
In the matter of manner* the Tennessee regiment
can compare with the one from New York and come
off winner.
The "Non-Partisans" ought to convene again long
enough to change their title to "Assistant Demo
crats."
THE BAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, OCTOBER 13, 1893.
JIM REA of Santa Clara, commonly known as
the "boss" of San Jose, has been giving evi
dence in the case of Jarman against Rea for
slander. He has made use of the occasion as an In
dian brave makes use oi the ceremony of the ghost
dance, to chant the war song of his deeds and his
virtues, to taunt his friends with over-candid words,
and to hurl a bully defiance at his foes.
Rea is not a poet, his song comes not in rhyme or
rhythm, but he displays such an exuberance 01 pic
turesque words and trumpeting phrases in the flow of
his swearfui prose as to prove he possesses a. true
dithyrambic fecundity. No one can listen to his
ghost dance song on the witness stand, or even read
it. without being aware that Jim needs only a higher
aspiration of soul and a better education to have been
quite a troubadour.
The reports of the proceedings of the trial tell us
that in giving his testimony 'Jim?y," as his loving
friends familiarly call him. "was very loquacious,"
and after a time his loquacity went so far "the court
was obliged to admonish the witness to confine him
seif to answering questions." The reports do not
tell us the e:tect of the court's action, nor was it
necessary they should. It goes without saying that
no admonition ever had any other effect on Jitnsy
than that of stirring him up and making him a little
inrlier and a little uglier than before.
When asked in what capacity he took it upon him
self to "run" the Common Council he chanted his
ghost song in this wise: "As a citizen of San Jose,
to protect the public treasury from the inroads of
scoundrels, and to see that honest and efficient people
are in public places. I never in my life received one
quarter of a dollar from any source whatever for the
appointment of any one. or for the advocacy of any
ire before a public body, and a man that does,
.pinion, is unht to be an American citizen."
A little later the brave went on to tell of a certain
job that had been put through the Common Council,
eh it appears some of the Councilmen got
a rake-off. He was then asked why he had not ex
posed that iniquity and prevented it. Whereupon he
sang in a minor key: "Oh, it was not a great wrong.
I did not consider it to be a great wrong. It was only
the amount of a few hundred dollars. I consid
ered it a wrong in the sense that it was being i
but I did not consider that from my position and the
position of my friends who are in this community it
would justify me in creating a public scandal, which
I would get the worst of in some way or other."
Then having in this belittling way made light of the
small steal and cast a pale shadow of suspicion on
his friend?, he resumed his louder tone and sang his
own praise again, while those shocked and astounded
friend? listened in amazement. "I am in politics,"
chanted Mr. Rea, "and whenever I locate a man who
if receiving money either as a public official or out
side of public office for his political influence. I mark
him about all the way from 75 per cent to zero, and
politics come up again I turn his picture to

A LESSON FOR ThE HOME MARKET.
A VALUABLE lesson for Pacific Coast people
was recently given to a reporter of the Port
land Telegram by a New York commercial
traveler engaged in selling Eastern goods in the
Western market. The commercial man stated he
could sell New York woolen goods in Oregon at bet
ter prices than the Oregon mills charge, notwith
standing the Oregon goods are the better.
When asked for an explanation of this seeming
contradiction of the rule that the best goods at the
lowest co<t always get the trade, the Xew Yorker
said: "There is a certain prejudice against Western
manufactured goods. Anything from the East is
supposed to be all right. Therefore shoddy goods
can be sold as long as they bear the Eastern trade
mark. It is fashion that rules the trade world. We
work the prejudice strong for all it is worth."
On this showing it will be seen the prejudice in
favor of Eastern goods is a very costly one for the
Western consumer. It would be cheaper to support
two vices than that one single prejudice. To main
tain it the customer mustj)ay the freight on Western
raw material across the continent, and the freight on
the manufactured goods back again, and finally a
higher price for the imported article than he would
have had to pay for a better one made by home man
ufacturers.
The lesson conveyed in this statement of facts,
while pertinent to the whole of the Great West, is
particularly so to California. No State in the Union
suffers more than this from the folly of patronizing
Eastern made goods and neglecting those made at
home. We ship to the East large quantities of raw
material â leather, wool, hops, fruit, etc. â and buy
back manufactured articles made of these by East
ern mills when the same classes of goods of erual
and often of superior quality are manufactured here
and can be bought for less money.
For the upbuilding of our great commonwealth
and for the advancement of its industries and the wel
fare of its labor there is necessary a change in the
attitude of the purchasing public toward local manu
factures. California should have a home market for
the products of its industry.
If there is to be any prejudice in trade it should in
cline to the side of the home product. That would
be the application of the great principle of protection
to private life, and the principle would prove as berle
ficial in that way as in national legislation. To pay
high prices for Eastern goods when Western goods
are better is a folly of which no intelligent man ought
to be gtiilty. It makes the New Yorkers laugh at us.
China's Empress seems to be a terror. If half the
stories told of her are true, she is a disgrace even to
the antiquated old dynasty which has for so many
years declined to progress. While the greed of the
powers in reaching for pieces of China is shameful,
the virago on the throne will kill off any tendency
toward sympathy.
In the matter of avoiding taxes the rich and highly
respectable New Yorker can easily give the rest of
the world points. However, in communities in which
false swearing is regarded as in bad taste, the method
might not work smoothly.
One mistake about the war investigation seems to
have been that Julian Hawthorne was not selected to
be all the witnesses and the entire jury. Still, the up
roar of Julian is almost unseemly.
Count Castellane seems to have a business turn of
mind and to be devoting its full energies to getting
some of the gold Jay Gould heaped up by years of
honest toil.
Zola's home has been saved to him. a circumstance
showing him to have some friends, which is more
than his persecutors can boast.

i ROMAN NTMERALS-Elaine. Elk, Cal.
This department has delved into ancient
and modern books in a vain endeavor to
discover how the Romans of ancient
times carried on their calculations with
the numerals. A French writer, who de- |
Clares that his hair turned gray in an
effort to ascertain how the calculations i
were carried on, wrote: "The Romans
assuredly had rules for calculating, but 'â 
they were for the simplest operation of I
the most terrifying complications." This
department has been unable to find any
mention of the use of fractions by the
Romans in their calculations, but if they
did use them then the complications must
have been more terrifying than those
suggested by the French writer. Five
hundred Is expressed by D. ISSS by ;
MDCCCLXXXVIII. and a million is ex
pressed by the letter M with a bar over i
it. The addition of these three sets of
numbers - would be represented by
MMDCCCLXXXVIII. the first M having '
i a bar over it. Another writer says that
"life is too short to attempt to ascertain ,
how the ancient Romans calculated, and '
it would be a useless waste of valuable
time as the cumbersome methods could
not be utilized." . . v
FITZGERALD'S
. GOOD RECORD
To the Editor of The Call: I Bee
with pleasure that in the inter- of
good government you urge the election
of General "William F. Fitzgerald, pres
ent Attorney General, to the place of
City and County Attorney of San Fran
cisco. It' ever a man deserved the
confidence and esteem of his country
men General Fitzgerald is thar*man.
When but 22 years of age. in the State
of Mississippi, he raised the standard
of the Republican party, and alone
went into every county in that Stati
championing the cause of Republican
ism, and he had the respect and es
teem of every man in the State.
He was nominated for Attorney Gen
eral against his wish, and had for his
opponent General Catching* one of the
foremost Democrats of the State, and.
although Catchinga was elected. Fitz
gerald ran 15,000 votes ahead of his
ticket so powerful was his oratory
arid so noble his conduct. He was
elected District Attorney of the \ lcks
burg District in Mississippi and se
lected almost without opposition. He
resigned and was appointed District
Judge In Arizona by President Arthur
without application on his part, and
with the earnest support of Senator
Laraar, a Democrat.
After a brilliant career there he some
sixteen years since came to Los An
geles, where he practiced law with
equal success for years.
He was appointed Supreme Court
Commissioner, as I happen to know,
without solicitation on his part or his
friends. He resigned that place to
practice law in San Francisco.
He was appointed to the Supreme
bench and was one of the best and
most popular Judges who ever sat on
that bench.
He was nominated and elected Attor
ney General, where be now is pursuing
a most distinguished and brilliant ca
reer.
He has never Bought office: it has
come to him without solicitation.
In the estimation of our most dis
tinguished lawyers and citizens he i 3
an id#al candidate for City and County
Attorney. A more honorable man. a
better citizen, a cleaner man in public
and private life does not live. I have
known and loved him since he was
9 and I 12 years of age. At this
time, when true patriotism should rule
the hour, this distinguished citizen,
able lawyer, brave soldier and pure
gentleman should receive the vote of
every true lover of his country.
H. S. FOOTB.
San Francisco. October 12.

OF THE FORESTS
-" The Call: Permit me to ex
my thanks for th- persistent man
ner in -which you advocate the protec
: <>ur forests from destruction by
fire. While camping near Mount Shasta
the past summer I was greatly im
pressed by the terrible effects of forest
fires. It ia bad enough to see the -
cut down so rapidly for lumber,
but it seems still Â« me I see the
rigs swept away by fire, leaving
the mountain* practically a desert.
By a wise supervision much of this
annual destruction could be prevented.
Forests might be separated into blocks
by the clearing of wide lanes, which
would act as barriers if a lire was once
startt- i N tksefl and warnings might
be posted extensively and fir<
might patrol the mure exposed d;
during the summer. I trust you will
kt-fp up the agitation and that th<* n^xt
iture Will take h-:<-;d of the :
in earnest. If our forests are burned
our State loses half of its wealth.
JOSIAH KEEP.

Court- for final settlement robbed the
prosecution vi the right of any appeal.

About 1 O'clock General King was

I anil was angry to white heat, in rela-
I tion to Merriam General King said that

General King had the two officers

j Concerning the affair General King
said at noon to-day: "I have hard ;
â¢' words to tell you how inexpressibly |

Wheelock was alone to blame. It is I

â  held responsible. Wheelock was on j

As I said before the responsibility was ,

countable for his actions. Having thus j
decided, I have ordered that Wheelock
! be relieved from duty and another man
put in his place."
"What does that amount to, general,
as a punishment?" was asked.
"Loss of pay and prestige for one
thing, besides the disgrace," was the
answer. "No, a court-martial does not
necessarily follow, nor Is it likely in this I
case. Unless some further complaint is !
made the matter may be considered as |
ended. I shall, of course, send a report
of the affair to Washington."

The United States transport Pennsyl
vania arrived in port laat night from

tioned being caused mainly through In
discretions of diet. The troop snips

through inadvertence. They were

The Supreme Court having decided
that the Council of State may decide
tho parlous question of what an "emer
gency" under our constituitfm" and one
within their power to appropriate, Mr.
Damon, the Minister of Finance, has
decided that there Is no necessity to
go before them for the amount with
which to purchase the site for the new
Reformatory School, as he will fund
the money until the next Legislature
ts and appropriates it. The know.
alls have an impression that Mr. Da
mon has high political aspirations and
know how to pull the wires. He pulled
a strong one on Saturday with his fete
champetre at his country residence at
Moanalua, given fn honor of the army
and navy. It was one of the most
successful garden parties or entertain
ments ever givpn here. He rallied
around him all the old regime for the
first time since the death of the mon
archy, as well as the representatives of
the new order. Unfortunately, Ad
miral Miller had sailed and Mr. and
Mrs. Sewall were out of town. Since
his visit to England Mr. Damon is much
impressed with the English baronial sys
tem, and consequently, assisted by thÂ«
President and Mr. Dole, he received in
a lordly and generous fashion with
special trains, bands dancing pavilions
and all that the heart could desire at
his country seat, which, in course of
time. Mill be a marvelously beautiful
spot, full of rare collections In botani
cal, zoological and historical treas
ures.
Considfrnble unpleasantness has been
caused by Minister Cooper and his Su
perintendent of Schools publishing a

school teachers and pupils to observe
October 19 as Lafayette day, and for
all the pupils to contribute as much a&
they can, or at least 1 cent, to the La
fayette memorial fund. This is re
garded by the poor Ha walla ns and the
children of all foreigners as being
rather Americanization by legislation.
Mnny children will ahsent themselves
from school on that day, although the
obnoXious circular has been withdrawn
from circulation.

ENCOURAGING
CONDITION OF
REAL ESTATE
Immediate Advance
Predicted.
MANY SALES NEGOTIATED
ASSOCIATION FORMED TO LESS
EN TAXATION.
Glances at the Records, New Build
ing Contracts and
General Realty
Notes.
The real estate market is still slowly
progressing, with every prospect of a
marked improvement before the end of
the present month. Although not as yet of
such a natuha as to bring universal
encouragement, still the advance made
is sufficient to make the brokers feel san
guine that the entire character of tne
"world of realty" will be changed from
long existent depression and a marked
tendency to deterioration, to a healthy
condition of progress before the lapse
of the remaining months of the present
year.
There is on foot a movement which, if
successful, will materially assist in bet
tering the condition of the realty market.
A new organization has been formed or
various real estate firms, the members of
which have subscribed to the following
resolution:
To the Voters and Owners of Real Property
of the city and county at ban Francisco:
Political parties in San Francisco nave be
come vile barterers. â 'â¢=>â '* : "
L,aws are not honestly enforced, the money of
the taxpayer Is squandered through the so
called political bosses that dominate the par
ties.
We believe the time for action has come;
that good citizens Irrespective of party fealty
should unite in a strong and determined ef
fort to suppress these practices by becoming
members of the Real Estate Agents" and Prop
erty Owners' Association.
This is a Keif-protecting proposition that re
quires our earnest attention; it "la a movement
at the present time that will mold the destiny
of this city. We want to place ourselves In
the hands of honest, reliable men and improve
the advantage that we now have within our
grasp. We should prepare ourselves tor the
reception of the new business opportunities
new thrown open to us by the now probable
acquisition of the Sandwich and Philippine
Islands also by the construction of the Nicara
gua canal. Wharves should be constructed for
the accommodation of commerce. Workshops
and factories should be constructed and oper
ated for supplying the wants of trade in thost*
countries.
Our desire is to create such conditions In
this city that all Idle houses shall be filled by
happy and prosperous families and .Induce idle
capital to invest here and in the vicinity. To
the above end you are invited to Join In con
vention to select such persons for office as will
bring the above much-desired results.
We, the undersigned property owners of this
city, pledge ourselves to become members of
the Real Estate Agents' and Property Owners'
Association, and we agree to meet In a con
vention to indorse worthy municipal candidates
from the nominations of the respective parties,
and promise to use our b<Â»st efforts In securing
the electlcn of such men as shall be Indorsed
by the Real Estate Agents' and Property Own
ers' Convention, which Is to be hÂ«ld on Octo
ber 10. 11 and 12. at a hall selected by the
president.
The obligation of this agreement is volun
tary; no fees are to be received for such serv
ices. With the object of good government we,
the undersigned, have subscribed our nam*a
to become members of this association.
J. K. Prior. John McNally, W. O. Watson,
A. F. Lcjeal. Ralph Harding. M. I. Johnson. |
I- H. Sly. A M. Speck. E. K. Clarke, Daniel |
Kelly. Charles Sladky. L. Metzger, E. L. At
kinson, Captain William Ed*, I. Harris, Ben
jamin Llchtensteln.
Since the organization was formed last
week at least 200 property-holders in the
city have signed the resolution.

I have a friend, in fact he is my brother-in-law, who is the outside
man for a New York crockery warehouse, a sort of salesman, purchas-
ing agent, collector and what not. He used to suffer from a catarrh
of the stomach. When he woke in the morning there would be a
gripping, a contraction, a tightness of the stomach, which, he said,
seemed to be clogged with a slimy, phlegm-like substance. Carlsbad
salts always did him some good, but now he uses a Ripans Tajbule. He
only takes one when occasion requires, just one now and then. When
this man, his name is Spalding, was asked to describe the effect of the
Tabule on him, he said : "Why, it brightens me up. It has an effect
like dry air; makes me feel something as I used to of a spring morning
when the air was crisp but tempered with sunshine. I don't know
how they produce such aa effect, but that iÂ§ the way the^ SPJJfJuXkW^"

COMMERCIAL WORLD.
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver lower.
Wheat firmer and Barley weaker.
The other cereals quiet.
Beans and Seeds dull.
Hay easier. Feedstuffs steady.
No change in Flour.
Potatoes and Vegetables in good supply.
Butter weakening. Eggs slow.
Poultrr steady.
Cantaloupes and Nutmegs dumped.
Wine Grapes arriving more freely.
Dried Fruits Blow.
Provisions weak and dull.
No further advance in Hogs,
Increased exports of specie.
THE NEW YORK MARKETS.
The circular of Henry Clews, under date of
New York. October S. says:
"The general commercial movement affords
no stimulus to the speculative spirit, but
rather causes some disappointment. Although
reports from the Western sections Indicate a
generally healthy condition of business and
foreshadow a pood aggregate for the fall trade,
yet there is a slowness and hesitancy tit move
ment that may be accounted for by the full
ness of the stocks of merchandise, which natu
rally produces a process of day to day buy
i")f â . turns out that the collapse In the ml.l
tary spirit consequent upon the close of the war
Ss accompanied by a corresponding abatement
of the speculative spirit, which, however
illogical it may seem, is by no means un
natural, for a relapse from any great excite
ment is apt to be attended by a general ieel
lng of quiescence. At the moment, the gen
eral situation seems to predispose the specu
lative Interest to taking a rest. The position
does not afford any strong inducements to
either bulling or bearing operations. ,
"The recent importations of gold have placed
the money market beyond any probability of
a relapse toward stringency. Owing to the low
rate of sterling exchange at Paris, It has been
found possible to ship hither about J2.5u0.0u0 of
gold from that city on London account, but ,
there is apparently little probability of any
further important importations of the metal
until there is an active revival of our exports
of grain, and, between the unsettled state of
the foreign wheat trade and the unwillingness
of our farmers to market their stocks, that
contingency can hardly be said to be within
eight. In any case, however, the condition of
the local banks has reached a point of plethora
In lending power which assures the money
market against any disturbance for the rest of
the year, if it does not foreshadow a period of
marked ease."

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, Oct. 12.â The undertone of
strength which has been manifest for several
flays assumed the upper hand in the stock mar
ket to-day, ignoring the continued weakness
In acme of the specialties and driving the bears
to cover, thus putting an end to the decline in j
Borne of these stocks. Buying to cover short I
contracts played no email part in the rise all
through the list. It -was not the result of an
awakened public Interest in the market but
â¢was rather by capitalists or groups of finan
ciers interested in this or that property ac
cording inside support. It was selling by In-
Side Influences that sent Northern Pacific down
last week and sympathetically affected the
railway list generally.
-day's sharp recovery In Northern Pacific,
which extended to 1%, was therefore a great
relief. There was conspicuous buying also in
Union Pacific preferred, the Grangers and
some of the Southern and Southwestern roads,
the net gains for many stocks reaching a
jiolnt. Kansas and Texas preferred was a con
epl<:ucus exception, with a net loss of nearly
a point. The speculation in Tobacco and Sugar
ctntinued active and feverish and the move
ments of these dominant specialties were con
flicting. While Tobacco bi-oKe 4% in the early
trading. Sugar made a sharp recovery of its
recent decline, and while Tobacco rallied nearly
to last night's close in the late dealings, the
early gain in Sugar was more than wiped out.
There was quite a striking movement in such
gilt-edged issues as Chicago and Alton and
Great Northern and St. Paul preferred. There
â was no conspicuous development in the gen
eral situation to account for the changed tem
per In Wall street, unless it might be the con
tinued heavy demand for cereals for export
end the advancing prices for them in foreign
markets. Money conditions In London were
alÂ«o slightly easier, which brougnt some buy
ing for London account and there was appre
ciable evidence of the Increased supply of r jr
j.lus capital here. The closing of the market
showed quite a firm tone in spite of weakness
in one or two of the Inductrials.
Railroad bonds improved in sympathy with
stocks and closed generally higher. Sales 12 -

% !i the bid price. Others were unchanged

LONDON' MARKET.
NEW YORK. Oct. 12.â The Evening Post's
London financial cablegram says:
The stock markets here opened steady to-day \
; but quickly gave way from lack of support and
the tone generally continued dull and lifeless.
Americans were especially good on New York
I buying and prices closed qtirel at the best.
Kaffirs were easy on German realizations.
I Insurance risks In securities are reported at
1 Lloyds against war with France at 10 guineas
per cent to the end of the year. These are i
really nothing but a few betting transactions ;
and Lloyds were so mistaken in their view as j
to the outbreak of the Spanish-American war
: that they are now Inclined to quote high rates.
French and German exchange on London Is
still rising. London discount* are -easier.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, Oct. ll.â Opening transactions In
Wheat were on a larger scale than the pit
has Been In a month. The strength at Liver
pool and the enormous cash grain business
done here yesterday was the all-Important con
sideration. The Liverpool advance was nearly
as great aa the rally here, and the seaboard
started In reporting further large export en
j gagements. Before the opening crush had sub
; sided the trade wan apprised of fifty boatloads
! taken at New York. To add to the strength
j which the market displayed, there were Eng
lish cables reporting It probable that Russia
would prohibit exports of cereals. Though
these were promptly denied, the trade _ had
plenty of other bullish news to stimulate it on !
the buying side. Minneapolis put her sales of
flour yesterday at 133.000 barrels. Broomhall
cabled that the Australian crop had been dam
aged by drought, and there was wet weather
In the Northwest which promised to cut oft to
some extent the arrivals in that quarter. With
all this, the temper of trade was skeptical.
Some of the important interests here seemed
to range on the selling side, and there was
fairly free selling from St. Louis. Local ex
porters were bearish and claimed that the ad
vances here had carried them out <jX line so far
as exports from Chicago were concerned. The
actual clearings for the day were 40,000 bushels.
Primary market receipts were 8,672.000 bushels,
against 1.357,000 bushels a year ago. Though
I sales of December were made early around 65@
65% c, an advance over last night's close of %>&
?4c. there was a backset to 64%Â®64%c before
noon. Directly after that time reports of ninety
; loads of Wheat and eighty-seven of Corn taken
1 for export at New York started the price up
I to 65%e again. The weekly as well as the dally
i calls were In this vicinity and considerable
: opposition was experienced. During the clos
ing, however, the reports of export business In
New York were swelled to 100 loads of Corn.
j The resulting excitement was Intense enough
. ' to carry December Wheat up to 65>Â£c, where j
i 1 It was met by offerings of numerous people who
1 ' had good profits and were willing to take them.
Though the New York stories were supple
mented with reports of 150,000 bushels cash
Wheat and over 1,000,000 bushels of cash Corn
disposed of here, It did not lighten the load of
those on whom the property had been dumped.
And in their efforts to shift the -weight the
, market was slumped down to 64% c for Decem
ber and thÂ« close was at 6i\L@M?ic, U@*Jc
lower. Another depressing feature near the
close was a. decline of 114 c per bushel at Buda- :
pest to-day and - <*> the paÃt three days.
An enormous export business and higher !
cables caused a strong feeling in Corn, . which
left off unchanged.
A scattered demand from scalper* and shorts
Improved Oats early. The market yielded,
however, with the slump In Wheat and lost He.
More reassuring yellow fever news from the :
South and an advance In the price of hogs at
the yards strengthened Provisions. Pork ad- 1

BOSTON WOOL MARKET.
BOSTON, Oct. 12.â American "Wool and
Cotton Review will say to-morrow:
The past week has been a very dull one.
Some negotiations have been pending which
may result in more business in the course of
a week or two, but the actual volume of sales j
has been even smaller than that of the pre- '
ceding week. One wool house reports that it :
could have done over $100,000 worth of business ,
If it had chosen to come down in Its asking
price, but it declined to do so.
Other Instances are cited Indicating the firm
ness with which wool is generally held by the
trade, although there are some spots where
holder* got more than they can take care of.
What demand there has been has mostly
ran to three-eighths and one-half washed
fleeces at 224j23c and Territory woola, the for- |
mer being used in army goods. Domestic fine I
wools have been very quiet, scarcely anything
having been done In this line of stock.
The sales of the week In Boston amount to
657 000 pounds domestic and 533,000 pounds for- !
eign, making a total of 1,190,000. against a total ]
of 1,543,000 for the previous week and a total
of 5,651.000 for the corresponding week last year.
Sales since January 1, 1898. amount to 88,112.
010 pounds, against 219,818,100 pounds last year
at the same time. -

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographic
Office, located in the Merchants' Exchange, ia
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of Interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
nulldin*. at the foot of Market street, is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon. 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived ea<-h day from the United States Signal
Observatory. Mare Island. Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any, is published
In the morning papers the following day.
CHAS. P. WELCH,
Ensign (retired), U. S. N., In charge.
TIME BALL.
Branch Hydrographic Office. U. S. N.. Mer
chants' Exchange, San Francisco, Cal., Oc
tober 12, IMS.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building was dropped at exactly noon to-dayâ
t. c.. at noon of the 120 th meridian, or at 8
o'clock p. m. Greenwich mean time.
V CHAS. P. WELCH,
Ensign (retired). U. S. N.. in charge.

XOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower low waters.

ALL CHARGES
OF PERJURY
DISMISSED
Grand Jury Work Goes
for Naught.
PROSECUTION IN DESPAIR
CANNOT CONVINCE TRIAL JU
RORS OF GUILT.
The Court Grants a Motion to Dis
miss and the Witnesses
Are Released From
Jail.
" Oakland Office San Franciepo Call,
908 Broadway, Oct. 12.
Constable Robert Dowling has nothing !
more to fear from the indictments al
leging perjury brought against him by the
Grand Miry. This morning, on motion of
the District Attorney, all the charges
were dismissed because of the failure of
juries to convict. When the cases were
called Mr. Snook informed Judge Ogden
that the former trials had been care
fully and earnestly conducted by the
prosecution, and that In each case the
jury had failed to convict. There is no
new evidence to place before the jurors,
and the inference being that they would i
fail to convict the third time, the Dis
trict Attorney could see no use in wast- ;
ing the time or the money of the State, j
Judge Ogdeu granted the motion to dls- I
miss.
Dowling was Indicted some months ago
on three charges of perjury. All of them |
were connected with swearing to bills I
against the county contracted in his ca- I
parity as Constable. When the first case '
was tried the defendant was represented [
by Attorney Emll Nusbaumer, and lie i
was acquitted. At the second trial two
weeks ago the jury disagreed, and it was
the retrial of the second case that was
before the court this morning.
The dismissal of the charges was wel
come news io the two tramps named
Kamsey and Carr. who gave the evidence ,
to the Grand Jury to furnish the basis
for the indictment. These two men have ,
b*>en held in custody since Dowling was '
Indicted, and to-uay they were released. '

HEW WATER
SCHEDULE IS
BEING TESTED
First Bill Under the
Woodward Rates.
PAYMENT IS DISPUTED
WORK OF LAST MARCH MAY
GO INTO COU3T.
One Water Company Attempts to Col
lect for What Was Formerly
Done Free for the
City.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Oct. 12.
About three weeks ago a story was
published In The Call to the effect that
the water companies of this city Intended
to put into use in the near future the
schedule for water rates fixed by the
'"solid six" of the City Council last March,
and which brought forth such a storm of
public indignation. The story was denied
at the time by the officers of one of the
companies. â *
Tills morning, when the Police and ire
Commissioners met, a bill was presented
for payment from the Contra Costa Water
| Company for changing and repairing fire
J hydrants as arranged for in the new
j schedule. This Is the first attempt made
to collect under this ordinance, although
it became legal July 1.
In the Woodward ordinance, which
raised such dissatisfaction, it is provided!
that the water companies should be paid
for repairing and putting in fire hydrants
ordered by the Council. In the ordinance j
of the previous year, which was passed
before the two companies had "agreed," !
the work of repairing and placing lire I
hydrants did not cost the city anything.
"City Attorney Dow read the new ordin
ance stating that the city was to stand
the expense, but Mayor Thomas raised
the question as to whether the ordinance
was in force. It had been passed by the I
Council, but had bourn vetoed by him and |
had i.' â  been passed over his veto. By a ;
shrewd piece of manipulation at the time
rates were being fixed, a resolution was |
passed fixing the water rates, as the
charter provides that they can be fixed by
either resolution or ordinance. The- water
companies will probably hold that it is
legal. The Items comprise two lire hy- ;
drants, costing $44 each, and a number
of repairs to other hydrants. After some
discussion, the bill was turned over to
the Chief of the Fire Department for an
explanation. ,
It is believed that this bill Is in the
nature of a test to- see if a fight will be i
made against the Woodward ordinance.
MERCHANTS WANT A
LOWER INSURANCE RATE
THE EXCHANGE WILL SEEK A
CHANGE IN THE LAW.
Director Sohst Declares That There
Is Discrimination Against
Oakland.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway , Oct. 12
The Merchants" Exchange of this city
is now attempting to Impress upon mem
bers of the Legislature the necessity or
some change in the law governing inaur- j
ar.ee companies.
The views of the exchange are clearly j
set forth In the following paper which j
was read at the regular meeting by Direc
tor J. ii. W. Sohst.
Oakland Is considered a gold mine by under
writer*, andithere ta no lovel too low for them; i
but clown, down into the pockets of their un
fortunate victims they g r > and dig up the jroi<S
with which they erect palaces In London, Llv
erpoolj I'a.rls, Hamburg, KrÂ«>mra, <-tc, and pay
la\i.Â«h salaries to their presidents.
The rates on business property are the same
as they were fifteen ears aEO, and in many
instances have been raised and may be called
prohibitory. People caimut insure, but have
to run their cvi) risk.
Do the underwriters K-ot that the people of
Oakland expend nearly SiOO.OOO a year for fire
protection? I don't 'think the - do. The
last time they pÃ'<l our fair city a visit
was when the yir"*t sprinkling was done
by drivers and horses of the fire department.
This state of affairs existed for economical
reasons, but with a team a half a mile away
from the engine house the Insurance companies
jumped up thfc rates. The fire horses have not
been ur.ed for street sprinkling for fifteen years.
The underwriters thould be officially notified
of the fact and be asked to oome down with

But the only way to loosen their iron frrip

struct our Senators nnd Assemblymen to pass

DENIED THAT HE
WAS THE DEAD MAN
O. F. KBIEG WAS NOT THE UN
KNOWN CORPSE.
A -Remarkable Case of Mistaken j
Identification Is Puzzling
Oakland's Coroner.
OAKLAND, Oct. 12.â The remains of |
the man recovered from the bay with a j
bullet wound through the head were ident- '
ified to-day by N. P. Reuss, the pro- !
prietor of bottling works, as those of
Oscar F. Krieg, an employe who worked ?
for him about two years ago, and resided j
in the Twenty-third avenue district. Dur
ing the afternoon four other men called
at the morgue and corroborated the ident
ification, all asserting positively that*
the dead man was none other that Krieg,
who of late has been a whisky drummer
for a San Francisco house.
To-night, to the surprise of Coroner
Baldwin and the morgue attendants, Os
car F. Krleg appeared personally and
positively declared that he was not the
dead man by any means, thus leaving
the Coroner still in the dark as to the
identity of the * genuine corpse. The
strong resemblance, however, is some
thing remarkable, but Mrs. Krieg and
four little Kriegs are happy in the j
thought that the husband and father is j
not he who lies cold on a Blab at the
morgue. â â¢?-* â -'
The watch of the deceased was exam
ined to-day by Jeweler T. M. Fry, 470
Thirteenth street, and he states It as his
opinion that the timepiece has recently
had a new case put on the old Waltham
works. The case is a gold-filled Cres
cent, and the works are fitted with the
Appleton & Tracy movement. This
might lead to an Identification.

THE SAN FRANCISCO Â« CALL, THURSDAY, OCTOBER 13, 1898.
OAKLANDERS
MAY HAVE AN
"OWL" FERRY
Further Facts About
the New Line.
PROPOSED CENTRAL DEPOT
A DIRECT ItOUTE TO THE RACE
TRACK.
Promoters of the Big Enterprise Only-
Desire the Fair Considei-ation
and Reasonable Support
of the Public.
"An owl boat" to and from Oakland is
a subject that has for years occupied the
3 attention of the host of people who travel
t between San Francisco and the "Modern
1 | Athens." This idea of the all-night boat,
- a convenience which has been long prayed
5 for without success, grows out of the ofll
â¢ cial announcement made by representa
f I tives of the Realty Syndicate on Tuesday
1 i evening to a meeting of property owners
3 of Golden Gate, that they intended to es
tablish a rapid transit, exclusive passen
ger service ferry between Emeryville and
1 â  San Francisco.
: If the big company, which has bought
i j up seven-tenths of the street car lines
' I of Oakland and Alameda, and about 4000
! j acres of land within and. outside of the
1 corporate limits of the sister city, could
[ satisfy the public that such a great lux
[ j ury as the night service would be inaugu
[ rated, they would find themselves sup
â¢ I ported by an army of friends, who would
' ' otherwise not be deeply Interested in the
â  j proposition. Such a service would, ac
. : cording to the judgment of those best
j calculated to judge, encourage a good,
â  I lucrative travel and popularize the new
j line to a great extent. It would receive,
; I not only the solid support of the over-the
! i bay people who patronize San Francisco
| playhouses and have to start for home In
â  : the middle of the last act, but the theater
; I managers would rise up and call the man
! agement blessed. Incidentally they might
j take a few blocks of stock just to show
â  their genuine sympathy with the enter
' prise.
! In any other city where such a large
i : population demands proper accommoda
tions, night boats are supplied as a mat
ter, of business and necessity. But the
Southern Pacific monopoly, which has
owned the bay since the Central Pacilic
was constructed, has considered its own
convenience and refused to run the owl
boats lest there might be an occasional
trip when the receipts would be a few
j nickelis shy.
A feature of the night ferry service
, which particularly interests San Fran
| ciscans is the fact that if such boats were
, run it would necessitate the operation of
j owl cars on this side of the bay in order
ito accommodate the traffic. Any Institu
tion that would guarantee such an in
' novation in this city could pass around
1 the hat and secure fat contributions to
its building fund. In other words the
: promoters of the new lerry wou'd be
given additional and substantial encour
agement if it were an assured fact that
the owl boats would be operated.
In speaking of the idea of an all-night
service yesterday, F. W. Beardslee, gen
eral agent of the Syndicate Company in
j Alameda County, stated that it was one
! that would be earnestly urged upon the
company when the time came. If the
proper support were given this could be
made a popular feature of the service.
| One of the suggestions in this connection
lie that with such boats there would be
hundreds of San Francisco night work
ers who would have their homes on the
, other side of the bay if they could de
pend on early morning ferry service.
With reference to the proposed "dis
| tributing depot," Mr. Beardslee stated
j that the Idea was to locate it on the
j northeast corner of Holden and Buena
Vista streets and to have it occupy oner
half a block. One of the contemplated
features would be an inner court, orna
j mented with flowers, a fountain, etc., and
jto be used as a lounging room. The
building would also be arranged with
I waiting rooms, a restaurant, etc., and a
: part set aside for stores. It is also pro
posed to run a special track to the Cali
i fornia Jockey Club's grounds. Passen
gers from the city would not have to
transfer, as the large cars to be used on
the mole would be run through to the
: track.
; An accommodation asked for by the
] people of Golden Gate Is the construction
of a short line through their section on
Tenth street, which would make a good
feeder. Another line which will be con
structed will be from the depot to West
Oakland to connect with the Eighth
Twelfth, Fourteenth and Sixteenth streets
lines of the Syndicate, as well as the one
!to Alameda. The Berkeley line a to con
nect with the Oakland Consolidated, and
the Piedmont stem is to be bo arranged
as to give almost a straight line to that
! picturesque resort.'
"This series of meetings we have
begun," said Mr. Beardslee, "is for
the purpose of letting the people of
Oakland know what wo are doing and
how we are doing it. We have one
: of the best business propositions to be
i found any Are, and all we need is to
have the public investigate it to be sat
is 0^ of its merits. We have about
$7,000,000 worth of property for which we
paid only $3,000,000. We are in Al shape
and making money. If the people of
i Oakland would look into these matters
: they could satisfy themselves that our
enterprise means a great deal to them
lln the development of the city. And if
they want this competitive, up-to-date
ferry service in a hurry they can have
it by showing that they take a substan
j tial interest in it. It rests with them
I whether the construction is tp begin "at
i an early date or three or four years
hence. However, I will reiterate my
I statement that this new ferry will b->
built."
The report of the meeting of property
I owners held at Golden Gate pub
lin yesterday's Call created much
discussion in Oakland yesterday A
strange feature of the situation over
there with reference to the proposed new
i ferry Is the fact that there are so many
people who put themselves out of the
way to decry the enterprise and destroy
the confidence of people who would oth
erwise give it substantial support.
It is well known in Oakland that this
"back-heeling" comes almost, entirely from
the Southern Pacific Company, which
; compels its agents and employes to use
: every effort to discredit any enterprise
that might affect the revenues of the
great monopoly and at the same time
â  give the public independence. The ad

The officers of the syndicate say that
they are not looking for fights or antag

business enterprise, In which they are
j distributing $1000 a day In Oakland. They
i ask only Just consideration and fair treat

SHOT WHILE AT A FUNERAL.
Peculiar Injury of T. J. Hamilton,
the Result of Firing a Volley
Over a Grave.
OAKLAND, Oct. 12.â T. J. Hamilton,
residing at Seventh and Oak streets, sus
tained a peculiar injury to-day while at
tending the funeral of a comrade, Joseph
Davis, a colored Grand Army man, who
dropped dead a few days ago.. The re
mains were interred in the soldiers' plat
in Mountain View Cemetery this after
noon. As is customary and to give such
funerals a military aspect three voUeys
were fired over the final resting place of
the departed colored veteran and com
rade.
With the last volley Comrade Hamilton
uttered a scream, for he had received the
full charge of wadding from one of the
guns In his left leg. He was escorted to
the Receiving Hospital where the wound
was dressed by Drs. Stratton and Tobin.
Oscar F. Krieg Identified.
OAKLAND, Oct. 12.â The remains of
the man recovered from the bay with a
bullet wound through the head have been
Identified at the morgue by N. P. Reuss,
proprietor of a bottling works, as those of
( tscar P. Krieg. who had been in his em
ploy for two years. Of late Krieg had
been a drummer for a San Francisco
wholesole liquor house. Krieg formerly
resided on Over street, near Allendale
avenue, but Reuss says that he had
moved to the Twenty-third avenue dis
trict, and that he leaves a wife and four
children. He is at a loss to know why
Krieg committed suicide.

LOVERS' KNOTS
AND BLOSSOMS
Brilliant Noon Ceremony
Which Unites Miss Eva
Moody and Clarence
Breeden.
i
In a bower of white chrysanthemums
and true-lovers' knots Miss Eva Moody j
and Clarence Breeden yesterday after- |
noon plighted their troth in the presence j
of about seventy invited guests.
The handsome home of the bride's par- j
ents, Mr. and Mrs. Joseph L. Moody, at i
the corner of Lombard and Jones streets, I
was the scene of the wedding ceremony.
The entire lower floor of the residence
was given over to those who assisted at |
the ceremony, and was profusely deco- i
rated with choice blossoms.
In the main drawing room, where the j
ceremony was performed, white was the |
prevailing tone of the decorations. The !
sitting room was in yellow and white. !
Red berries and ferns Â«vere gracefully ax- j
ranged in the hallway, and the dining \
room was in pinkâ American. Beauties !
and cosmos prevailing.
The large porch was inclosed In canvas, j
and here were arranged the tete-a-tete !
tables where the seventy guests sat down
to a bridal luncheon.
Exactly at noon the bridal party en
tered the drawing room. It was led by ]
the bridesmaids, Miss Eva Madden, Miss I
Bernie Brown and Miss Bertha Dolbeer. j
They were gownf-d alike in frocks of j
whit? moupseline de soie over slips of
green taffeta. Large bouquets of Amer- |
ican Beauties completed the costumes. |
Next came the maid of honor, Miss Mai ,
Moody, a sister of the bride. She wore |
a dainty dress of white dotted chiffon
over white taffeta silk. The bouquet she I
carried was composed of bridesmaid I
roses.
Following came the bride, leaning upon J
the arm of her father. She looked ex- i
tremely pretty in her wedding dress of
white uuchesse satin. The skirt was en
trainÂ© and elaborately trimmed In point
d'esprit lace arranged to form lovers' i
knots. The high-necked, long-sleeved
bodice was shrouded in white tulle and i
was finished with satin stock and belt, j
The tulle veil was attached with a spray i
of orange blossoms, and the bridal bou- j
quet was of lilies of the valley.
A* the bridal party reached the bower
it was mot by the groom, his best man,
Edward M. Beck, and the officiating
clergyman. Rev. Mr. Shaw of St. Luke's I
Church. , !
After the solemn service was over con- i
gratulations were in order, and later the |
guests eat down to a bridal breakfast, I
during which the bride and groom were '
toasted again and again.
Mr and Mrs. Breenden left last even
ing for Southern California, where the
honeymoon will be spent. On their re
turn they will reside at the Richelieu,
on Van Ness avenue.
The Pullmans Given Time.
The Railroad Commissioners met yester
day afternoon, the only absentee being
Commissioner Clark. I. H. Polk, expert
of the board, was also present. The spe
ciaJ object of the meeting was to investi- I
gate the affairs of the Pullman Palace
Car Company, which has been subpenaexl
to produce its books and make a showing
of its affairs before the board.
Several letter* were read showing the
great interest the investigation has arous
ed in different parts of the country. One j
of these, from J. I>. Miller of Wisconsin,
is as follows: "I notice your efforts to
reduce the rates in Pullman cars. Any
person can buy a berth in a Pullman from
Laredo to Monterey, Mexico, from 4
p. m. to 8 a. m., 10 hours, for 90 cents.
Also, Xl Paso to Mexico City, 48 hours, or
J224 miles, for $4; and the Pullman are
putting on cars in Mexico, whenever tho '
opportunity present* Itself. If the Pull
man can do this to Mexico, they can do it
to California.
"Four years afiro the railroad company
charged per tourist cars, per berth, with
the earne equipment as now $3, Omaha to
California. As soon as Pullman took
charge the rates were raised to $5."
A letter was also received from Talcott
Williams of the Philadelphia Press, ask
ing for particulars of the investigation.
T. C. Coogan, local attorney for the
Pullman Company, said that while hÂ© did :
not know what action the company would
take in the matter, he believed that it
would not contest the authority of the
board. Though the sentiment of the board
was against much further delay, Mr. Coo
gan was finally allowed two weeks in
which to find out the attitude of the Pull
man directors in Chicago, after which the
meeting adjourned.
An Eye to Improvement.
L. M. King, superintendent of the street
department of the Merchants' Association,
returned yesterday from a six weeks'
trip to the East, where he had been sent
by the association to gather information
us to the methods adopted in the larger
cities on the Atlantic side in the matters
ot sewerage, garbage, pavements, etc.
Mr. King: has visited every city of note
and he states that in very many respects
San Francisco is far behind the times.
However in none of the municipalities did
he find everything- perfect. One city would
excel in its sewer system, anothor in its
pavements and another in the matter of
aisposiÃg of its prartiago. He will have :
many valuable suggestions to make for
the benefit of this city in matters when
the association makes recommendations
to the Supervisors for public improve
ments.

AMENDMENT NUMBER SIX.
(Being Assembly Constitutional Amendment
No. 35.)
A resolution to propose to the people of the
State of California an amendment to section
six, article nine of the Constitution of the State
of California, relating to grammar schools, by
which it is proposed to amend said section to
read as follows:
Section 6. The public school system shall in-
clude primary and grammar schools, and such
high schools, evening schools, normal schools
and technical schools as may be established by
the Legislature or by municipal or district
authority, hut the entire revenue derived from
the State school fund and the State school tax
shall be applied exclusively to the support of
primary and grammar schools. Grammar
schools shall Include schools organized in a
school district, or union of school districts,
having more than one thousand Inhabitants, in
which a course of study shall be taught which,
will prepare pupils to enter the agricultural,
mining or scientific department of the Univer-
sity of California.

shall commence at twelve o'clock meridian on.

must adjourn to some date not less than thirty
nor more than sixty days from the time of ad-
journment. If the two houses fall to agree
upon a time at which they will resume their
session, the Governor shall, by proclamation,
fix a date for such reconvening, which shall be
within the limits above prescribed. Upon re-
assembling the Legislature shall complete its
session. No pay shall be allowed to members

no bill shall be introduced )o #/ther nous's exÂ«
cept at the first twenty-ftÂ»Â» days of the session,
without the consent of three-fourths of the

ENDS HER WAYWARD
LIFE WITH POISON 1
Mrs. Nora Robbe, a Principal in
Two Fresno Elopements,
Commits Suicide.
FRESNO, Oct. 12.-Mrs. Nora Robbe,
whose name had become familiar to every
one In the county on account of her nu
merous escapades, brought her erratic life
to an end this afternoon by taking- a dose
of carbolic acid. In the forenoon she ap
peared to be in say spirits and chatted
with friends. At 3 o'clock In the after
noon she took the poison in the Park
lodging-house. She told a companion
Minnie Lowery, that she had taken the
deadly potion, but It was thought she was
Joking. When it was learned that she had
attempted suicide, two physicians were
summoned. They could do nothing for
her, and she expired at about 4 o'clock.
Mrs. Robbe formerly was Nora Hutchin
son, and she had respectable relatives
living here. Two years ago she married
Robbe, being then only 17 years old.
While her husband was temporarily away
from the city she eloped to Jamestown.
Tuolurnne County, with his brother. mc
runaway couple were brought back, but
shortly afterward she ran away again
with her brother-in-law.
Several months ago she made an effort
to reform, and joined the Volunteers of
America, but she soon relapsed into way
wardness. About two months ago her
husband brought suit for divorce, and
since then Mrs. Robbe has been going a
rapid pace.
FREIGHT WRECKED
NEAR TEMPLETON
Engine and Cars Go Down an Em
bankment and Four Men
Are Scalded.
TEMPL.ETON, Oct. 12.â N0. 34. west
bound freight train on the coast division,
â was wrecked at Havel, six miles south
of here, fhis morning. The engine and
several cars left the track on a sharp
curve, rolling down an embankment fifty
f <â '':. high. The tender and three cars
jumped over and lay ahead of the en
gine.
Engineer Mclntyre, the fireman and two
brakemen were on the engine at the time
and were badly scalded. Engineer Mc
lntyre was scalded about the head, and
his head was badly cut. Brakeman Clem
Powers was badly scalded. ThÂ« fireman
and the other brakeman were not serious
ly Injured, The men were taken to San

was cleared nt a late hour this afternoon.

THE SAN FEANCISCO CALL, THTJRSPAT, OCTOBER 13, 1898.
McKINLEY AT
THE EXPOSITION
Enthusiasm Shown by
the People.
CROWDS CHEER PRESIDENT
DELIVERS AN ADDRESS EX
PRESSING HIS GRATITUDE.
Unstinted Patriotism Displayed Dur
ing the Third Day's Cele
bration of the Peace
Jubilee.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
OMAHA, Oct. 12.â The third day of
Omaha's Peace Jubilee, which was
"President's day" at the Trans-Mlssls
sippl Exposition, opened with lowering
sky. About 10 o'clock, however, the
sun burst forth In all its radiance,
making the hearts of thousands of peo
ple glad. It was after 10 o'clcok when
the Presidential party, escorted by
President Wattles and members of the
entertainment committee in carriages,
started for the exposition grounds.
They were headed by a platoon of
mounted police, while the President and
party were escorted by the Board of
Governors of the Ak-Sar-Ben, mounted
and dressed in their regalia. The party
proceeded directly to the grand plaza
on the bluff tract of the exposition,
where on the music pavilion the formal
exercises of the day occurred.
President McKinley probably never
received a more enthusiastic greeting
than that which awaited him at the ex
position grounds. By far the greatest
crowd in the big Western show's his
tory, so large a crowd that its num
bers are almost impossible to be es
timated, thronged about the huge plat
form from which be made his address.
Hardly one sentence was spoken by
him which did not evoke from the peo
ple cheers upon cheers. After Presi
dent Wattles had introduced the dis
tinguished guests of the exposition it
was fully five minutes before the sub
sidence of the applause permitted the
President to begin speaking. Follow
ing are extracts from his address:
Gentlemen of the Trans-Mississippi Ex
position and Fellow-Citizens: It is with
genuine pleasure that 1 meet once more
the people of Omaiia, whose wealth
of welcome is not altogether unfamiliar
to me. and whose warm hearts have be
fore touched and moved me. For this re
newed manifestation of your regard and
for the cordial reception of to-day my
heart responds with profound gratitude
and a deep appreciation which 1 cannot
conceal, and which the language of compli
ment is inadequate to convey. My greet
ing not alone to your city and the State
of Nebraska, but to the people of all the
States of the Trans-Mississippi group par
ticipating, and I cannot withhold congrat
ulations over the evidences of their pros
perity furnished by this great exposition.
If testimony were needed to establish the
fact that their pluck has not deserted
them, and that prosperity is again with
them, it is found here. This picture dis
pels all doubt.
In an age of expositions they have added
yet another magnificent example. The
historical celebrations at Philadelphia and
Chicago and the splendid exhibits at New
Orleans, Atlanta and Nashville are now
a part of the past, and yet in Influence
they still live, and their beneficent results
are closely Interwoven with our national
development. Similar rewards will honor
the authors and patrons of the Trans-
Mlssissipp] Exposition. The contribution
will mark another epoch in the nation's
material advancement.
Continuing, the President referred at
length to the lessons of patriotism
taught by the late war, paying glowing
tribute to the gallant service of the
country's army and navy.
The inspiring conclusion of the
President's address wa.s the signal for
a Bcene of wild acclamation.
Bowing and smiling to the crowd of
enthusiastic constituents the President
Bat down and gave way to Postmaster
General Smith, whose speech came
next on the programme. Mr. Smith's
reception was almost as fluttering rb
that given to the President. He spoke
briefly along lines similar to those fol
lowed by his chief, dwelling particu
larly upon the magnitude of the expo
sition and the marvelous energy and
genius which the great West had de
voted to its consummation.
After Mr. Smith had concluded the
surging throng of humanity around the
platform clamored for more. Loud calls
for General Miles, Senators Allen and
Thurston and Governor Holcomb were
heard, and they advanced in succession
to the front of the platform and In re
sponse to the deafening cheers of the
people bowed and smiled their ac
knowledgments, but did not make any
remarks. Then the Chinese Minister,
the Korean Minister and Senor Quesa
da, the Cuban emissary, were brought
forward in response to the demands of
the crowd. Patriotic selections by the
band closed the exercises on the plat
form.
JESSE JAMES HAS A
FRIEND ON THE BENCH
Released by Order of a Circuit Judge,
but Promptly Rearrested by
the Police.
KANSAS CITY, Oct. 12.â .Tesp* J.-imeÃ,
the 21-year-old son of the notorious bandit '
of border days In Kansas and Missouri,
who was arrested yesterday for com- '
pllelty in the recent Missouri Pacific train
robbery, was brought before circuit .
Court Judge Henry this morning under |
habeas corpus proceedings, and the court
ordered his release. Judge Henry scored
the prosecuting attorney and the police
for having permitted the arrest without a
warrant. Ex-Governor Crlttenden. who !
was in the courtroom, denounced the ar- i
n hi of young James as a "greater crime ]
than train robbery."
The police put on a bold front, how
ever, and immediately after James was ;
released by Judge Henry to-day he was
arrested on a State warrant. Later i
James wns released under $25tX) ball.
W. E. Lowe, Andy Ryan and at least !
one or two other suspects are also under !
nrrest. Andy Kyan is a brother of Bill !
Ryan of the "old James gang, and was un
til recently in the Kansas City Pire De- |
j>ar!ment.
The police state that Lowe has made a
full confession, implicating James, Ryan |
and others and that they will be able to
Froduce evidence fully corroborating ;
jowe's statement in essential points.
ROYALTY STARTS
FOR THE HOLY LAND
BERLIN, Oct. 12.â The Kmperor and
Empress of Germany started at 8 o'clock
tbifl morning on their journey to the Holy
Land. They will go direct to Constanti
nople and thence to Palestine. The Im
perial party is also accompanied by a
body of gendarmes and by eighteen
equerries. The .jendarmes have been
taught photography, and, by the Em
peror's order, will photograph everything:
of Interest.
There are 110 trunks In the baggage cars,
many of them of Immense Blze and con
taining the dresses of the Empress. One
enormous box, which does not leave the
Emperor's vicinity, is in charge of a high
functionary. It contains valuable gifts
and decorations valued at 4,000,000 marks,
for Oriental officials.
Only six horses were taken and they
are for the Emperor's personal use.
The Sultan of Turkey bought thirty
six carriage horses in Berlin for the use
of, the Empress of Germany and her suite.
CALIFORNIA KNIGHTS
RECEIVE VISITORS
Keep Open House at
Their Headquarters.
EASTERN TEMPLARS GREETED
PBOCEEDINGS OF THE GRAND
ENCAMPMENT.
One Proposed Amendment to the
Constitution Imposes Conditions
on Membership in the
Commanderies.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PITTSBURG, Oct. 12.â This afternoon
the headquarters of California Com
mandery No. 1, in the Carnegie build
ing, were thrown open to all visiting
Knights Templar. To-morrow had been
originally chosen as the day for the big
reception by the Golden State Knights,
but in especial deference to Detroit vis
itors and others who wanted to leave
the city to-night the Californians de
cided to have open house this after
noon. .Long before the doors opened
there was a big crowd on the sidevsalk
in front of the headquarters and a
steady stream poured in and out of the
handsomely decorated rooms until
nightfall, when the reception came to
an end and the headquarters were
closed.
To-morrow a general reception to uni
formed Knights and their ladies will be
given, and in the evening there will be
a special reception. The Grand Com
mandery, assisted by California Com
mander; No. 1, will receive the officers
of the Grand Encampment, after which
the headquarters of California Com
mandery No. 1 will be closed.
With the exception of receiving the
members of Detroit and other com
mauderies that had to leave for home
before to-morrow nothing was done by
the Californians to-day. A few of
them spent part of the day visiting the
headquarters of other commanderies,
and in the evening th.-y went to the ball
of the Tancred Commandery in the
courthouse. Many of them will leave
for home to-morrow night. The party
will split, some going East to visit rela
tives, while others will journey h<mie
to California leisurely. The California
delegates are pledged to Louisville for
the next conclave.

PROCEEDINGS IN THE
GRAND ENCAMPMENT
riTTSBURG, Oct. 12.â The weather
to-day was very line. The effects of
yesterday's showers are all obliterated
and the successful carrying out of the
different features of the lengthy
Knights Templar programme for to
day was assured. During the day an
exhibition drill took place on the race
track in Schley Park.
The Grand Encampment Knights
Templar resumed its meeting to-day In
Carnegie Hall. Past Grand Master
James H. Hopkins of Washington, D.
C, proposed two amendments to the
constitution. In one It was proposed
to amend section 3 of article I, so as
to read: "A stated conclave shall be
held triennially in Washington, D. C,
on the second Tuesday of October, un
less the Grand Encampment shall at a
Stated conclave designate some other
time and place."
The other proposed to amend section
1. article I, by placing the Grand Pro
late next in line after the Grand Junior
Warden.
An amendment was also offered by
Past Grand Master William R. Bowen
of Nebraska to the code of st.-ttutes as
follows: â Membership in a command
ery Is dependent upon and coexistent
with membership in a lodge of Free
Masons. Any companion of the Red
Cross of Knights Templar who re
mains for six months unafflllated with
a lodge of Free Masons is lpso facto
siiHsÂ»nded in Knight Templary, such
suspension to be terminated by affilia
tion with a lodge."

PROPOSALS^
PROPOSALS Invitedâ Sealed proposals, will be
received for channel corrections on the San
Joaquln River and Stockton Channel, San
Joaquin County. California. The San Joaquin
River work embraces channel corrections at
proposed cut-offs Nos. 3 and 4, as shown on
sheet 3 of surveys on file in this office,
and to be made in accordance with plans and
specifications now on file In this office.
Bids will be received at the office until 1
o'clock p. m.. WEDNESDAY, November 2,
1898, at which time they will be publicly
opened. - _ *
Each bid must be accompanied by a certified
check on some bank of good standing, payable
to the order of H. J. Corcoran, chairman of
the Auditing Board to the Commissioner of
Public Works of California, in .the sum of
two thousand ($2000) dollars, which check
shall be forfeited to the State If the bid which
such check accompanies is accepted and the
party making the bid shall refuse to enter
Into a contract and execute a satisfactory
bond for the faithful performance of tha con-
tract awarded him. The right to reject any
and all bids is reserved.
All bids must be directed to "Auditing
Board, room No. 61, Capitol building. Sacra-
mento. California." indorsed "Proposals for
Channel Corrections San Joaquln River and
Stockton channel."
By order of the Auditing Board.
JOHN MARKLEY, Secretary.
Sacramento, October 5, 1898.
