WILLIAMS' RECORD
IN THE WINDY CITY
CHICAGO, Oct. 12.â Charles A. White,
alias Williams, who was engaged to
marry a Fan Francisco belle and heiress,
is well known as t>nderloin society Is
known, and some years ago had quite
a reputation as a rounder and money
spender among the demi-monde. At that
time he was supposed to be a Wyoming
rancher, and his periodical visits were
looked forward to with delight by saloon
keepers and disreputable women, with
whom he usf-d to consort on those occa
sions. Among the women favored by his
attentions was Zoe Owens, then reigning
queen of her class, upon whom he lav
ished cheap affections and costlier jewels.
He established her in sumptuously fur
nished apartments on Jackson boulevard
and Michigan avenue, and on several oc
casions took her to his Wyoming home.
His relations with her were kept up for
three or four years, when he suddenly
disappeared, his whereabouts remaining
a mystery even to the Owens woman,
who subsequently learned through a third
party that her erstwhile friend, whom she
spoke of to associates and Intimates as
"Prince Charlie," was in South Africa.
Soon after getting this information Mi.ss
Owens left Chicago without warning, and
it was supposed she too had gone to the
diamond fields, but about a year ago she
was found to have taken up her residence
in Brooklyn.
SENTIMENT OF PEOPLE
WHO ENTERTAINELHIM
In social as well as business circles the
Williams exposure continues to be the
sole topic of interest. It is a subject
which unites all classes on a common
platform that has for its insignia the all
powerful love of what Is timely news. *
Reflection and reason have brought a
new actor into the stirring social drama,
and into what now must be turned the
second act of the powerful domestic play.
Williams is relegated to second part, and
the man who is responsible for his in
troduction assumes the leading role.
"We do not care who this man â or,
what his name â White, Williamsâ is,"
says the select circle In which the alleged
mine-owner heretofore moved. "We don't
know him. Hut we do know youâ at least
we hope we do, and we want you to ex
plain what you meant by Introducing such
a character into our homes."
This asking for an explanation, how
ever, seems to be merely a matter of
form. Translated from the misleading
language of the select into serviceable
and sterling English, It says: "Mr. Spon
sor, socially speaking, you are a dead
man. Don't come and see us any more."
In direct measure as the sympathy for
the impoÃed-upon beautiful girl who was
to have been Williams' wife increases,
contempt and loathing for the impostor
augments. The friends and admirers of
the fair fiancee suffer with her, and yet
are cruelly denied the comfort that openly
expressed sympathy might bring. An in
nate sense of delicacy prevents them call
ing upon her, and written words are too
cold to picture the depths of the sincere
affection all those who have had the hap
piness of knowing her feel.
The outside world, the cruel observing
outside world, looks on at this drama
that almost proved a tragedy, and pro
pounds conundrums that no one can and
no one attempts to guess. Among other
things it asks: "Are there really dia
mond mines and millions galore?" "Will
the wedding ever take place?" And last
but hardly least. "What are they going to
do to Williams social sponsor?"

PICTURES TELL A
STORY OF DECEPTION
"Words may be juggled until they can
be made to fit. almost any required ver
sion of a tale. The camera, however,
bears mute testimony that cannot be con
troverted. The criminal and the crim
inal hunter acknowledge this fact.
When Mr. Williams posed before the
camera in Chicago to gratify the whim
of the exacting- Zoe Owens, little did he
think that that portrait, designed to
adorn the demi-monde's boudoir, would
find a resting-place in Plnkerton's rogues'
gallery.
Another unfortunate circumstance con
nected with the useful art of photog
raphy, from the criminal's point of
view, is the facility with which reproduc
tions are made. Mr. Williams, like
others, has cause to regret the fact. In
every branch office of the Pinkertons
throughout the world Williams' picture
Is tiled away against the day It may be
needed.
Williams' life, with Its series of pyro
technical displays and dramatic episodes,
is not one easily relegated to the recesses
of obscurity. They leave their Impress.
The Pinkertons, whose boast is that they
never sleep and hardly ever forget, need
no references to recall Williams' car' :r.
In 1592 Williams had his photograph
taken to distribute along the tenderloin.
A comparison of his appearance then
with his appearance now shows the grad
ual progression from the gold brick man
to the swell society impostor. They in
dicate the change of tactics with the
change of coat. The sealskin and expan
sive, shirt front of 1593 calculated to Im
press and capture the rural visitor is re
placed in 1898 by the Tuxedo, bait for
higher and more worldly game.
The first step into the real world was
his introduction at the Rand Club, Jo
hannesburg. South Africa. Adapting
himself to his surroundings, he gradually
doffed the chrysalis and came forth, the
butterfly. If those who knew him while
in Chicago could now meet him here they
would find In his changed appearance
much at which to marvel. He now pre
sents the well-groomed appearance of the
man of the world. It is a far, cry from
the sealskin overcoat of the habitue of
the Chicago tenderloin to Tuxedo even
ing coat of the present time.
DETECTIVE PINKERTON
VOUCHES FOR STOLL
"In the statements made for publica
tion by Williams," said Detective William
A. Plnkfrton yesterday, "he attempts to
cast a slur upon the character of Attor
ney W. K. Stoll of Cheyenne.
"I have had business dealings with Mr.
Stoll for twelve years," continued Mr.
Pinkerton, "and have found him a cap
able, a fearless and an honest man. He
bears .the highest reputation throughout
Wyoming, lie was District Attorney of
Cheyenne and was a bright, intelligent
official. Williams cannot hope to
strengthen his case by attacking tho
probity of that man."
PREPARING FOR THE
POLICE COURT ELECTION
CITY CHARTER AMENDMENT TO
BE VOTED UPON.
A Lively Contest Expected at the
Polls Next Saturday Between
Opposing Factions.
BERKELEY, Oct. 12.â A special elec
tion for the residents of the university
town has been called by the Board of
Trustees for next Saturday, October 15.
The election is for the purpose of deciding
whether or not Berkeley shall have a
Police Court. It will take the form of
voting upon an amendment to the city
charter, providing for the establishment
of a local Judiciary having jurisdiction
over violations of city ordinances and
minor misdemeanors.
The measure, however, is likely to en
counter a very serious opposition. The
bone of contention is an article of the
amendment calling for the appointment of
the Police Judge by the Board of Trus
tees. A very strong faction has devel
oped which is bent upon opposing this
system of appointment. They charge the
Trustees and the Town Attorney with
trying to railroad through a scheme
which shall make the Police Judge' a mere
tool in their hands.
The Trustees, individually, who passed
the amendment, the Good Government
Club, the North Berkeley Improvement
Club and the residents or the East End
generally are known to be strongly in
favor of the provision just as it stands.
The opposition is confined to certain sec
tions or the West End and the South End.

OVERDUE
STEAMERS
REACH PORT
The Australia Arrives
From Honolulu.
SUDDEN DEATH OF FRENCHY
_
HIS TAXING OFF MOURNED BY
THE STEAMER'S CREW.
Hard Luck of Captain Gibson of the
Schooner Dux*bury in Getting
Rid of a Load of
Hay.
A fleet of vessels, all of which had
made long passages, arrived in port yes
terday. Among them was the steamers
Australia from Honolulu, City of Pan
ama from Central American ports and
the Orizaba from Mexican ports. The
Australia was delayed by a slight break
down in her circulating pumps, the City
of Panama was detained at one of the
Guatemalan ports, but the Orizaba ar
rived on time.
Among the fleet were many coasters
and the ship Invincible from Pyramid
Harbor with 39.672 cases of salmon, the
bark S. C. Allen from Honolulu with
sugar and the ship I'ort Patrick from
Newcastle, Australia, with coal. The
Allen was beaten nine Uays In the run
up by the bÃrkentlne S. N. Castle, which
made the passage in sixteen days, while
the Port Patrick has beaten the ship
Springbank and bark Holliswood from
Australia. The Holliswood Is now out
eiffhty-tWO days, and the Springbank
seyenty-three days, while the Port Pat
rick made the voyage In sixty-eight days.
The crew of the Australia was In
mourning- when the vessel docked yes
terday. The day after leaving Honolulu
"Frenchy," the ship's mascot, died and
was buried at sea. Frenchy was the
sucking pig secured at the island of
Guam, where the Australia touched on
her way to Manila with troops. The
little animal became a great pet, and was
taught a number of tricks. After its
death the carpenter made a coffin for It,
and the chief officer placed the following
Inscription on it: "S. S. Australia, sth
October, 189$. Died of peritonitis and
pleuro-pneumonia on board the S. S.
Australia at sea in lattUide 23 degrees
3S minutes north, longitude 154 degrees 40
minutes west, the much beloved pig
Frenchy, after a long and painful illness,
and was buried at sea the same day, as
you or I would be, and not without a
human tear, either, my friends."
Before the shotted coffin was dropped
overboard Chief Officer Lawless dellv-
THE DUXBURY'S HARD LUCK.
She left here over a week ago with a
load of hay for the Berkeley stockyards, and
is still stuck in the mud with half her cargo
aboard. In order to get rid of the hay and
get the vessel back to San Francisco Captain
Gibson has been compelled to lighter the
stuff in Whitehall boats.
ered a eulogy before the assembled pas
sengers and crew. The following are
Purser McComb's notes of the speech:
"Frenchy was born on the beautiful Isl
and of Guam, one of the Ladrones, about
' June 1 fast, and was there captured
! three weeks later by United States troops
while on a foraging expedition. Being of
a tender age Frenchy was raised on a
bottle, and it was not long before her
quaint manners endeared her to the en
tire crew of the Australia, more particu
larly to George Kittle, the carpenter, who
fed and cared for her. During her Ill
ness he made a bed of banana Teaves for
her, and stayed by her to the last, and
her spirit has passed over to him who
notes the sparrow's fall.
"Who dares to say, 'It did not?'
"What proof have you, mortal, that the
most insignificant of God's creation is not
endowed with the spirit which is the hie?
"The life of all things, animate and in
, animate, are of God, and his spirit alone
! can create life and he It Is who orders the
! Pale Angel to bring that life back to him:
; and so this pig, who owes its creation ana
life to him, also owes its death to him.
"Mortal, don't get a big head because
i you're not a pig, for you may be one and
not know It. Elevated as you are in let
ters, music, hypocrisy and pride, you may
forget how much more God expects of
you. being 'lord of creation,' than if you
were only one of the rank and iile of
creation. â¢ * *
"My mortal brother, you and I were
I ushered Into this world without our con
j sent. Some day, my brother, we will be
I ushered out of it in the same way. We
Ido not know whence we came,
neither do we know whither we are going;
so get your head coopered, keep it nor
j mal and don't deny that there may be a
place of rest for the birds of the air, tne
i beasts of the field and the fishes of the
j sea."
The next day after the funeral the
steamer was hove-to for repairs, and a
big shark came around. The crew began
fishing for him, thinking- he might hive
Bwallowed "Frenchy," coffin and all.
When caught he measured 9 feet 9 inches,
but when dissected no trace of the pig
was found.
While at Honolulu the troopship Penn
sylvania arrived from Manila, and was to
leave for San Francisco a couple of days
later. She will probably get nere about
Saturday, and as the Zealandiu is due
from Nagasaki on that date also, there
will be seven transports here getting
ready to take out troops. The ship Ta
coma will also probably sail a"-am for
Manila with horses. If she does, a new
donkey-engine will have to be put in, as
the one now on the vessel, which waa put
in by the Government, is too small.
The City of Panama brought up nine
cabin and six steerage passengers irom
Central America. Among the former .la
General L. Letona of San Salvador, The
general has come up here in connection
with his mines, for which he wants some
: machinery. Carlos Ezeta, ex-President
of San Salvador, and General Letona are
bitter enemies, and should the two meet
there will be trouble. On his return to
San Salvador General Letona will be a
candidate for the Presidency.
Captain George Gibson and the gasoline
schooner Duxbury are in trouble. Overs
week ago the vessel started for the Â«erKe
ley stockyards with a load of hay, ana
she still lies on the mud flats, only half
discharged. Captain Gibson soon found
that he could not get up to the wharf,
and, after waiting four days for a mgn
tide, he tried to warp his vessel off into
deep water. This he failed to do, as the
Duxbury was hard and fast. After sev
eral attempts at getting the vessel off , he
came over to San Francisco and. securing
a Whitehall, went back and be Sf n n v "|Â£^:
ering the schooner. By Saturday next
Captain Gibson expects to get jrid of his
load, and the Duxbury will then go bacK
into her usual trade. ...
Peter Carvones has severed his connec
tions with the Sausalito steamers. In
future John D. Nield will look after the
restaurants. For twenty-one years Mr
Nield has been baggage master of the
North Pacific Coast road, and before that
was for nine years butler for t M Â°.
Latham. He says he will make the din
ing-rooms of the Sausalito and San Ka
fael the best on the bay.
TEMPLARS OBJECT TO
THE ARMY CANTEEN
GRAND LODGE PETITIONS THE
PRESIDENT TO ABOLISH IT.
Graduates From the Course of Study
Read Essays During the Second
Day's Convention.
The Grand Lodge of the Good Templars
met a* a committee of the whole yester
day morning and appointed a committee
on finance and a committee on the plan
of campaign to be carried out during the
coming year. The committee on finance
Is composed of F. E. Coulter, W. P. Neth
erton, A. A. Smith, John Offlt, Volney
Taylor, George D. Kellogg and W. I.
Hull. The campaign committee is com
i <.f F. E. Coulter, J. M. Walling,
Theodore D. Kanouse, Mrs. E. M. North.
J. W. Webb, Mrs. Schadt, Miss Ida Tally
and A. B. Taynton. These committees
will report to the committee of the whole,
which will convene oner more before the
session of the Grand j^ouge concludes.
It was decided that on Saturday next
the Grand Lodge should visit the Or
phans' Home at Vallejo.
A resolution referring to the army can
teen was proposed by Rev. J. \V. Webb
and was adopted. It was as follows:
Whereas, A loud and heartfelt cry against
the enticing, debasing, crime-causing and dis
ease-breeding army cant. -ens has gone up to
! the President of the United States from temp
' frame people, churches, bishops and many of
' the soldiers and not a few of the command-
Ing officers; therefore, be it
Resolved. That this Grand Lodge most earn
estly adds its appeal to President McKlnley as
the commander-ln-chief of the army, a patriot
and a Christian, to upe his undoubted authority
to abolish the canteen system.
A copy of the resolution will be sent to
Washington.
The memorial committee reported obit
uary notices on Mrs. Hat tie A. T. Tarlow,
Bertrand R. Fairweather, William C.
Barren, Mrs. Lva C. Armstrong and John
Taylor.
A report on the Juvenile work was read
by Mrs. M. E. North, superintendent of
that work, and was referred to a future
meeting of the Grand Lodge.
In the evening another entertainment
was given. The class of instruction has
Just finished its three years' course and
essays were read by those who have
profited by the excellent literature sent
out by the organization. The essays were
as follows: "Cracking Knots," F. A. An
thony; "Our Volunteer Army," Mrs.
Clara Crowhurst; "The Chautauqua So
ciety and Its Work," Mrs. E. M. North;
"Value of a Course of Study," George D.
Kellogg; "Satan in Society," Mrs. G.
Schadt.
Addresses were made uy Theodore D.
Kanouse, Rev. Philip Graif, Professor
William Crowhurst and William H.
Barnes. Musical numbers were rendered
by Mrs. Knell, Mrs. Morse and Miss F. Irene
Phelps. and the audience sang several
patriotic airs under the leadership of Mr.
Irwin. The convention will meet again
this morning, when omcers will be elected
for the ensuing year.

BOXING AND WRESTLING.
Al Neil Had an Easy Thing Stop
ping a Portland Dub at the
Merchants' Club.
The Merchants' Athletic Club grave Its
monthly boxing entertainment last even
ing and the house was packed with sports
and politicians. Maurice Lee and Steve
Vicine opened the evening's fun with an
exhibition of wrestling which was very
clever. Then followed the boxers.
Mike Mollls, a lad who knows very lit
tle about fisticuffs, but is a game young
ster, made a chopping block of Frank Ely
for a couple of rounds, but he tired per
ceptibly, and in the fifth round Ely took
courage and closed in upon his opponent
Mollls was too weak to stand the attack
ami after receiving a few hard smashes
in the face he squatted on the carpet and
remained down until counted out.
The event of the evening was a fizzle
Young Fitzsimmons, the alleged welter
weight champion of the Northwest
proved himself a dub of the lowest grade'
He was boomed by "Dub" Evans as be
ing "a coming man," possibly because he
may have bested Evans, ana that would
not be any feather in his cap. Hbwever
the Young Fitzsimmons stood half a
round in front of A<i Neil, who hammered
him all over the ring, and was about to
give "Fitz" the sleepy punch when the
police stopped the very much one-sided
game.
Boxing clubs Bhould see their talent i
tried out before they match them.
TEACHER SOPER
HAS SKIPPED
FOR MONGOLIA
i He Defrauded Four
: Brokers.
i SCHOOL BOARD DROPS HIM
; FACTS CAME OUT AT YESTER
DAY'S MEETING.
â  I
Plans for a Noe Valley Schoolhouss
Adopted, but Action on.
the Construction
Delayed.
A sensation developed at yesterday's '
| meeting of the School Board, when, with
! out warning, a resolution was presented
dropping 11. F. Soper from the roll of
teachers "for non-compliance with the
rules." The resolution was adopted with
out discussion, a statement of the facts
leading up to the resolution having been
! privately made to the members of the
I board In secret session as a committee
jof the whole. The story, as told by one
of the members of the board, Is substan
tially as follows:
Soper, who was a teacher In the Ham
i ilton Evening School, had left town,
after having swindled a number of war
rant brokers to the tune of several hun- :
dred dollars. He had gone to these per
sons and made out orders for his August
and September salaries, selling the
same at a small discount and pocketing
the cash. He sold his order for his $50
August salary to Solomon Kampe and
again to James F. Snelling. The same
tactics were followed on a more exten
sive scale with his September salary of
$50, which was in turn sold to Solomon
Kampe, August Weihe, James F. Snell
ing and the San Francisco Collateral
Loan Hank, each receiving an order on
the Treasurer lor the amount. Kampe
happened to present both orders several
days ago in advance of the other holders
and the money was paid to him without
a suspicion that fraud 1 had been com
mitted. Latter the regaining orders
turned up at the office of the clerk of the
Board of Education, and the swindle was
exposed. Inquiry for Soper revealed the
fact that he was missing. - It was sub
sequently reported that he had left on j
an Asiatic boat in the capacity of a
waiter.
The question of appealing from the re
cent decision of the Superior Court, rein
stating K. C. Kilpatrick as prlncipu.l of
the Business Evening School, furnished
opportunity to Director Gallagher to be
rate the Judge who rendered the decision.
"It was simply rotten!" shouted Gal
lagher, "and the Juilpe was biased by po
litical reasons. It was the most shame
less decision ever rendered in a California
court. We should appeal:" The board
coincided with Gallagher and decided to
file an appeal with the Supreme Court.
Another important matter was the se
lection of plans for a line new school
building at Twenty-third and Douglass
streets. The successful competing archi
tect was T. J. Welsh. His plans pro
vide for a twelve class room build
ing two stories high, with B]
rooms for principal, teachers and schol
ars. The building is s* arranged that all
of the class-rooms have an eastern ex
posure and plenty of light. The estimated
cost of the structure is Â£>0,000.
Members of the board stated after the
meeting that there was a strong probabil
ity that the new school building would not
be constructed this year, owing to lack of
funds. The matt' r has not, however,
been finally settle I
Miss Agnes McLa Jin was transferred
from the John Swett Grammar School and
elected principal of the new school at
Forty-third street and Point Lobos
avenue. Mrs. S. W. McPherson. teacher
of mathematics in Polytechnic High
School, was chosen head of the mathe
matical department of the same school.
C. H. Crowe!! of the Mission High S
was transferred to the High School Class
of the Horace Mann Evening- School.
Miss Rose Ryan of the secondary sub
stitute list, was elected a regular High
School teacher and assigned to the place
vacated by C. H. CroweU.
The board discussed at some length the
recommendation of the committee on
evnning schools that Myrtle Cerf, an ex
tra substitute teacher of bookkeeping in
the Spring Valley School, bo assigned as
teacher of said class, and that L. A.
Ramus, an extra substitute teacher of
bookkeeping In the Hamilton Evening
School, be assigned as teacher of said
class.
Director Head entered a violent pro
test, which was seconded by Carew,
against appointing any more teachers
when the board is confronted with a de
ficit in the school fund. Head read a
statement showing that the board has
already expended during the present
school year $254,267. and that at the pres
ent rate of expenditures there will be a
deficit at the close of the year of $200,0)0.
He stated that the board now has left
out of its appropriation a sum that will
only permit of the expenditure of $85,000
per month for the remainder of the year,
whereas the present salaries and ex
penses aggregate $93,500 per month. By
a vote of six to five the board decided not
to appoint the two teachers.
The four conspirators charged with
defrauding the city through false and
fraudulent bills presented to the School
Board for lumber, surrendered them
selves at the City Prison yesterday morn
ing. They had previously prepared thom
selvrs with bondsmen, and were promptly
released each on $1000 bonds.
Director T. A. Burns had as his sure
ties F. B. Lorigan. 843 Castro street and
Charles Campodonico. 1411^ Mason street
John J. O'Brien, Inspector of buildings'
had David Salfield, 61S Broderick street'
and P. F. Nolan, 812 Market street'
George S. Graham, assistant inspector"
had Thomas D. Riordan, 828 Montgomery
street, and School Director Burns Ed
ward D. Swift, contractor, had Edward
F. Franks. 9S Fillmore street, and F C
Mack, 423 Waller street. Burns and
O'Brien's bonds were accepted by Clerk
Kohlmann of Judge Mogan's court-
Swift's by Clerk Hayes of Judge Jo
aohimsen's court, and Graham's by Judge
Low.
The cases will be called in Judge Low's
court this morning, but a continuance
will probably be asked and granted.
